Calling Rebels Before Fighting: Ruling and Conditions
Stating the recommended procedure of calling rebels back to obedience before fighting, and defining when rebels are not considered baghat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on calling baghat rebels to obedience before fighting and identify the condition that removes a group from the category of baghat.

Core Idea
Calling rebels before fighting is mustahabb, not wajib, based on 'Ali's example at Harura'.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the procedure an imam should follow when dealing with rebels (baghat). The Hanafi school, relying on the action of 'Ali (RA) at Harura', holds that calling them back is recommended but not obligatory. The passage also clarifies when a group with a clear grievance is not considered baghat at all, and touches on the role of shubhah in takfir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the imam calling rebels to return to obedience before fighting. According to the Hanafi school, this is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib (obligatory).
The text explicitly says: 'yubda'u bihi istihbaban la wujuban'.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the action of 'Ali (RA) with the people of Harura' (the Khawarij) before the battle of Nahrawan. He called them and cleared their doubts.
Dalil: sunnah fi'liyyah (action of a caliph).
3
Understand the wisdom of istihbab
Calling them is the 'easier of the two options' and might repel evil. If fighting without calling is also valid, calling is not required.
The imam is not sinful if he fights without calling, because the rebels already know the situation.
4
Identify the exception: when rebels are not baghat
If the rebels present a clear and unambiguous injustice (la shubhata fihi) causing them to rebel, they are not considered baghat. Muslims must help them against the imam.
This contrasts with when their claim is dubious (mushtabih).
5
Apply the shubhah principle to takfir
Those who have a doubt (shubhah) based on ijtihad, even if their belief is kufr in essence, are not declared kafir. Extremists without ijtihad are ruled kafir.
The text gives examples: deniers of ru'yah, 'adhab al-qabr, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (خرج قوم مسلمون عن طاعة الإمام وغلبوا على بلد دعاهم إليه وكشف شبهتهم بأن يسألهم عن سبب خروجهم فإن كان لظلم منه أزاله، وإن قالوا الحق معنا والولاية لنا فهم بغاة لأن علياً رضي الله عنه فعل ذلك بأهل حروراء قبل قتالهم ولأنه أهون الأمرين ولعل الشر يندفع به فيبدأ به استحباباً لا وجوباً فإن أهل العدل لو قاتلوهم من غير دعوة إلى العود إلى الجماعة لم يكن عليهم شيء لأنهم علموا ما يقاتلون عليه فحالهم كالمرتدين وأهل الحرب بعد بلوغ الدعوة ؛ كذا في العناية. فلو أبدوا ما يجوز لهم القتال كأن ظلمهم أو ظلم غيرهم ظلماً لا شبهة فيه لا يكونون بغاة ولا يجوز معاونة الإمام عليهم حتى يجب على المسلمين أن يعينوهم حتى ينصفهم ويرجع عن جورهم بخلاف ما إذا كان الحال مشتبهاً أنه طلب الحق لكن جزمهم ببطلان الصلاة خلفهم لا يصحح هذا الجمع اللهم إلا أن يراد بعدم الجواز عدم الحل مع الصحة وإلا فهو مشكل ؛ هكذا ذكره الشيخ كمال الدين بن الهمام. وعلى هذا يجب أن يحمل المنقول على ما عدا غلاة الروافض ومن ضاهاهم فإن أمثالهم لم يحصل منهم بذل وسع في الاجتهاد فإن من يقول بأن علياً هو الإله أو بأن جبريل غلط ونحو ذلك من السخف إنما هو متبع أسوأ حالاً : محض الهوى وهو ممن قال ما نعبدهم إلا ليقربونا إلى الله زلفى فلا يتأتى من مثل الإمامين العظيمين أن لا يحكما بأنهم من أكفر الكفرة وإنما كلامهما في مثل من له شبهة فيما ذهب إليه وإن كان ما ذهب إليه عند التحقيق في حد ذاته كفراً كمنكر الرؤية وعذاب القبر نحو ذلك فإن فيه إنكار حكم النصوص المشهورة والإجماع إلا أن لهم شبهة قياس الغائب على الشاهد ونحو ذلك مما علم في الكلام. وكمنكر خلافة الشيخين والساب لهما فإن فيه إنكار حكم الإجماع القطعي إلا أنهم ينكرون حجية الإجماع باتهامهم الصحابة فكان لهم شبهة في الجملة وإن كانت ظاهرة البطلان بالنظر إلى الدليل فبسبب تلك الشبهة التي أدى إلها اجتهادهم لم يحكم بكفرهم مع أن معتقدهم كفراً احتياطاً بخلاف مثل من ذكرنا من الغلاة فتأمل اهـ.
His statement: (A group of Muslims went out of obedience to the imam and overpowered a town. He calls them and clears their doubt by asking them the reason for their rebellion. If it is due to an injustice from him, he removes it. If they say: 'The truth is with us and the authority is ours,' then they are rebels, because 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) did that with the people of Harura' before fighting them, and because it is the easier of the two options, and perhaps evil will be repelled by it. So he begins with it as a recommendation, not an obligation. For if the people of justice fought them without calling them to return to the community, there would be no blame on them, because they know what they are fighting over, so their case is like apostates and people of war after the da'wah has reached them; as in al-'Inayah. If they present what justifies fighting—like their oppression or the oppression of others with an injustice that has no doubt—then they are not rebels, and it is not permissible to assist the imam against them; rather, the Muslims must help them until the imam treats them fairly and returns from his oppression. This is contrary to when the situation is dubious that they seek the truth, but their certainty about the invalidity of prayer behind them does not validate this combination; unless what is meant by impermissibility is the lack of validity despite correctness, otherwise it is problematic. Thus stated by Shaykh Kamal al-Din ibn al-Humam. On this, what is transmitted should be understood as applying to other than the extreme Rafidis and their ilk, for such people have not exerted their effort in ijtihad. The one who says that 'Ali is a god or that Jibril made a mistake and similar foolishness is merely following pure desire. He is among those who say: 'We only worship them to bring us nearer to Allah.' It cannot be expected from the two great imams (Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i) that they would not rule that such people are among the worst of disbelievers. Rather, their statement applies to those who have a doubt (shubha) in what they hold, even if what they hold is in itself kufr upon investigation, like deniers of the vision, punishment of the grave, etc. For in that there is denial of well-known texts and consensus, but they have the doubt of analogizing the unseen to the seen, and similar things known in theology. Also like the denier of the caliphate of the two shaykhs and the one who insults them, for in that there is denial of definitive consensus, but they deny the authority of consensus by accusing the Companions, so they have a doubt in general, even if it is outwardly false in view of the evidence. Because of that doubt, to which their ijtihad led, they are not declared disbelievers, even though their belief is kufr, as a precaution. Unlike those we mentioned from the extremists. So reflect. End.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage provides the practical procedure for dealing with rebels (baghat) based on the example of 'Ali (RA) with the people of Harura' (the Khawarij at Nahrawan). The imam is instructed to first ask the rebels why they have rebelled. If they claim oppression from the imam, he must remove that oppression. If they claim that the truth is with them and that they have the right to rule, then they are indeed rebels (baghat), and the imam should call them back to the community. This call is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib (obligatory), because if the imam fights without calling them, he is not sinful since the rebels already know the imam's authority. The text then adds a crucial condition: if the rebels have a legitimate grievance that is clearly unjust (with no ambiguity), then they are not considered rebels at all, and the imam is not to be helped against them; rather, the Muslims must help the rebels to obtain justice. However, if the situation is ambiguous, e.g., they claim to seek truth but also declare praying behind the imam invalid, this does not justify joining them. The passage then transitions back to the issue of takfir, repeating that those with a shubha (like deniers of certain doctrines) are not declared kafir, but extreme Rafidis who lack any ijtihad are clearly kafir.

Key Terms
دعوة (da'wah): The call to rebels to return to obedience before fighting them.
شبهة (shubhah): An ambiguous situation or doubtful interpretation that prevents takfir or changes the ruling.
استحباب (istihbab): Recommendation; an act that is encouraged but not obligatory.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on the imam calling the rebels (baghat) to return to obedience before fighting them?
A) It is wajib (obligatory)
B) It is mustahabb (recommended)
C) It is haram (prohibited)
D) It is sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that calling rebels before fighting is mustahabb, not wajib, and groups with a clear and unambiguous injustice are not considered rebels.

Definition of Bughat and the Ruling on Takfir
Defines bughat and discusses the ruling on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of bughat and explain why they are not declared disbelievers (kafir).

Core Idea
Bughat are Muslims with a doubt; they are not declared kafir, even if their beliefs constitute kufr.

The Hanafi school treats rebels (bughat) as Muslims whose blood and property are protected, provided they have a plausible misinterpretation (shubha). This lesson establishes the definition of bughat, distinguishes them from the Khawarij, and explains the critical principle that takfir (declaring disbelief) is avoided when a shubha exists. We draw on Ibn al-Mundhir's report of consensus and the hadith of al-Hadrami to ground this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Define 'bughat' and 'shubha' from the passage. Note that bughat are Muslims who rebel against the just Imam but do not go as far as the Khawarij in spilling blood.
'Shubha' is a plausible but incorrect interpretation that prevents takfir.
2
Understand the takfir debate
Ibn al-Mundhir states that no jurist agrees with the hadith scholars on declaring the Khawarij disbelievers, implying a consensus of the fuqaha. The passage also mentions that some jurists do declare some innovators as kafir, but only when they oppose a definitive proof without a valid shubha.
The first report (no takfir by jurists) is more established.
3
Distinguish bughat from khawarij
The Khawarij make lawful the blood and wealth of Muslims, while bughat do not. However, bughat are still considered Muslims due to their shubha. The text calls the equation of bughat with Khawarij in al-Bada'i as 'deficient'.
Both groups have a shubha, but the Khawarij are more extreme in practice.
4
Apply the shubha principle
Even if a rebel's belief is inherently kufr (e.g., denying the vision of Allah), if they arrived at it through a plausible analogy (shubha), they are not declared kafir. This is a precaution (ihtiyat). Exceptions are the ghulat of the Rafidah who follow pure whim with no ijtihad.
The Hanafi school is lenient in considering shubha to avoid takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير باب البغاة
ابن المنذر : لا أعلم أحداً وافق أهل الحديث على تكفيرهم، وهذا يقتضي نقل إجماع الفقهاء . وذكر في المحيط أن بعض الفقهاء لا يكفر أحداً من أهل البدع وبعضهم يكفرون بعض أهل البدع وهو من خالف ببدعته دليلاً قطعياً ونسبه إلى أكثر أهل السنة والنقل الأول أثبت. نعم يقع في كلام أهل المذاهب تكفير كثير لكن ليس من كلام الفقهاء الذين هم المجتهدون بل من غيرهم ولا عبرة بغير الفقهاء، والمنقول عن المجتهدين ما ذكرنا وابن المنذر أعرف بنقل مذاهب المجتهدين، وما ذكره محمد بن الحسن من حديث الحضرمي يدل على عدم تكفير الخوارج. وأما البغاة فقوم مسلمون خرجوا على الإمام العدل ولم يستبيحوا ما استباحه الخوارج من دماء المسلمين ذراريهم اهـ. فما في البدائع من تفسير البغاة بالخوارج فيه قصور وإنما لا نكفر الخوارج باستحلال الدماء والأموال لتأويلهم وإن كان باطلاً بخلاف المستحل بلا تأويل . قوله : (خرج قوم مسلمون عن طاعة الإمام وغلبوا على بلد دعاهم إليه وكشف شبهتهم بأن يسألهم عن سبب خروجهم فإن كان لظلم منه أزاله، وإن قالوا الحق معنا والولاية لنا فهم بغاة لأن علياً رضي الله عنه فعل ذلك بأهل حروراء قبل قتالهم ولأنه أهون الأمرين ولعل الشر يندفع به فيبدأ به استحباباً لا وجوباً فإن أهل العدل لو قاتلوهم من غير دعوة إلى العود إلى الجماعة لم يكن عليهم شيء لأنهم علموا ما يقاتلون عليه فحالهم كالمرتدين وأهل الحرب بعد بلوغ الدعوة ؛ كذا في العناية. فلو أبدوا ما يجوز لهم القتال كأن ظلمهم أو ظلم غيرهم ظلماً لا شبهة فيه لا يكونون بغاة ولا يجوز معاونة الإمام عليهم حتى يجب على المسلمين أن يعينوهم حتى ينصفهم ويرجع عن جورهم بخلاف ما إذا كان الحال مشتبهاً أنه طلب الحق لكن جزمهم ببطلان الصلاة خلفهم لا يصحح هذا الجمع اللهم إلا أن يراد بعدم الجواز عدم الحل مع الصحة وإلا فهو مشكل ؛ هكذا ذكره الشيخ كمال الدين بن الهمام. وعلى هذا يجب أن يحمل المنقول على ما عدا غلاة الروافض ومن ضاهاهم فإن أمثالهم لم يحصل منهم بذل وسع في الاجتهاد فإن من يقول بأن علياً هو الإله أو بأن جبريل غلط ونحو ذلك من السخف إنما هو متبع أسوأ حالاً : محض الهوى وهو ممن قال ما نعبدهم إلا ليقربونا إلى الله زلفى فلا يتأتى من مثل الإمامين العظيمين أن لا يحكما بأنهم من أكفر الكفرة وإنما كلامهما في مثل من له شبهة فيما ذهب إليه وإن كان ما ذهب إليه عند التحقيق في حد ذاته كفراً كمنكر الرؤية وعذاب القبر نحو ذلك فإن فيه إنكار حكم النصوص المشهورة والإجماع إلا أن لهم شبهة قياس الغائب على الشاهد ونحو ذلك مما علم في الكلام. وكمنكر خلافة الشيخين والساب لهما فإن فيه إنكار حكم الإجماع القطعي إلا أنهم ينكرون حجية الإجماع باتهامهم الصحابة فكان لهم شبهة في الجملة وإن كانت ظاهرة البطلان بالنظر إلى الدليل فبسبب تلك الشبهة التي أدى إلها اجتهادهم لم يحكم بكفرهم مع أن معتقدهم كفراً احتياطاً بخلاف مثل من ذكرنا من الغلاة فتأمل اهـ.
ظلم مثل تحميل بعض الجبايات التي للإمام أخذها وإلحاق الضرر بها لدفع ضرر أعم منه ؛ كذا في فتح القدير قيد بإسلامهم لأن أهل الذمة إذا غلبوا على موضع للحراب صاروا أهل حرب كما قدمناه لكن لو استعان أهل البغي بأهل الذمة فقاتلوا معهم لم يكن ذلك نقضاً للعهد كما أن هذا الفعل من أهل البغي ليس نقضاً للإيمان فحكمهم حكم البغاة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. يعني بالتبعية للمسلمين فلا يرد على التقييد بالإسلام والمراد بالإمام السلطان أو نائبه. قال في الخانية من السير قال علماؤنا : السلطان من يصير سلطاناً بأمرين : بالمبايعة معه ويعتبر في المبايعة أشرافهم وأعيانهم، والثاني أن ينفذ حكمه في رعيته خوفاً من قهره وجبروته، فإن بايع الناس ولم ينفذ حكمه فيهم لعجزه عن قهرهم يصير سلطاناً، فإذا صار سلطاناً بالمبايعة فجار إن كان له قهر وغلبة لا ينعزل لأنه لو انعزل سلطاناً يصير بالقهر والغلبة فلا يفيد، وإن لم يكن له قهر وغلبة ينعزل .اهـ. وقيد بغلبتهم على بلد لأنه لا يثبت حكم البغي ما لم ينغلبوا ويجتمعوا ويصير لهم منعة ؛ كذا في المحيط. ولم يقيد المصنف الإمام بالعادل وقيده في فتح القدير بأن يكون الناس به في أمان والطرقات آمنة. قوله : (وبدأ بقتالهم) يعني إذا تعسكروا واجتمعوا وهو اختيار لما نقله خواهر زاده عن أصحابنا أنا نبدؤهم قبل أن يبدؤنا لأن الحكم يدار على الدليل وهو الاجتماع والامتناع. وهذا لأنه لو انتظر الإمام حقيقة قتالهم ربما لا يمكنه الدفع فيدار على الدليل ضرورة دفع شرهم. ونقل أنه لا يبدؤهم حتى يبدؤه فإن بدؤه قاتلهم حتى يفرق جمعهم، وظاهر كلامهم أن المذهب الأول. وفي البدائع : يجب على كل من دعاهم الإمام إلى قتالهم أن يجيب ولا يسعهم التخلف إذا كان له غنى وقدرة لأن طاعة الإمام فيما ليس بمعصية فرض فكيف فيما هو أبو حنيفة من الاعتزال في الفتنة ولزوم البيت محمول على ما إذا لم يدعه أما إذا أدعاه الإمام فالإجابة فرض .اهـ. وأما تخلف بعض الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فمحمول على أنه لم يكن لهم قدرة وربما كان بعضهم في تردد من حل القتال، وما روي «إذا التقى المؤمنان بسيوفهما فالقاتل والمقتول في النار (۱) محمول على اقتتالهما حمية وعصبة كما يتفق بين أهل قريتين أو محلتين أو لأجل الدنيا والمملكة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي المحيط : طلب أهل البغي الموادعة أجيبوا إن كان خيراً للمسلمين كما في أهل الحرب ولا يؤخذ منهم شيء فلو أخذنا منهم رهوناً وأخذوا منا رهوناً ثم غدروا بنا وقتلوا رهوننا لا ينبغي لنا أن نقتل رهونهم لأن الرهون صاروا آمنين في أيدينا وشرط إباحة دمهم باطل، ولكنهم يحبسون إلى أن يهلك أهل البغي أو يتوبوا، وكذلك أهل الشرك إذا فعلوا برهوننا ذلك لا نفعل القدوري
Book of Siyar, Chapter of Bughat. Ibn al-Mundhir said: I know no one who agreed with the hadith scholars on declaring them disbelievers, and this implies transmission of consensus of the jurists. It is mentioned in al-Muhit that some jurists do not declare anyone from the people of innovation a disbeliever, and some declare some of them disbelievers, which is one who opposed with his innovation a definitive proof, and he attributed it to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, and the first report is more established. Indeed, much takfir occurs in the speech of the people of the schools, but it is not from the speech of the jurists who are the mujtahids, but from others, and non-jurists are not considered. And what is transmitted from the mujtahids is what we mentioned, and Ibn al-Mundhir is most knowledgeable in transmitting the schools of the mujtahids. And what Muhammad ibn al-Hasan mentioned from the hadith of al-Hadrami indicates the non-takfir of the Khawarij. As for the bughat, they are a group of Muslims who rebelled against the just Imam and did not deem lawful what the Khawarij deemed lawful of the blood of Muslims and their offspring. So what is in al-Bada'i' from interpreting bughat as Khawarij is deficient. Indeed, we do not declare the Khawarij disbelievers for deeming blood and wealth lawful due to their misinterpretation, even if it is false, contrary to one who deems lawful without interpretation. His statement: (A group of Muslims left obedience to the Imam and overcame a land; he calls them and exposes their doubt by asking them the reason for their rebellion. If it is due to oppression from him, he removes it. If they say, 'The truth is with us and the leadership is ours,' then they are bughat, because Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, did that with the people of Harura before fighting them, and because it is the easier of the two matters, and perhaps evil will be repelled by it. So he begins with it as recommended, not obligatory. For if the people of justice fought them without calling them to return to the community, there would be nothing upon them, because they know what they are fighting for, so their case is like apostates and people of war after the invitation has reached them. Thus in al-Inayah. If they present what permits them to fight, such as if he oppressed them or oppressed others with an oppression having no doubt, then they are not bughat, and it is not permissible to help the Imam against them. Rather, it is obligatory upon the Muslims to help them until he treats them justly and returns from his injustice, contrary to when the case is ambiguous that he sought the truth. But their certainty of the invalidity of prayer behind them does not validate this combination, unless by 'non-permissibility' is meant non-permissibility with validity, otherwise it is problematic. Thus mentioned by Shaykh Kamal al-Din ibn al-Humam. Upon this, what is transmitted should be interpreted as excluding the ghulat of the Rafidah and those similar to them, for their like have not exerted effort in ijtihad. Indeed, one who says that Ali is God or that Jibril made a mistake and the like of such nonsense is only following the worst case: pure whim, and is among those who said, 'We only worship them that they may bring us near to Allah.' So it is not conceivable from the two great Imams that they would not rule that they are among the most disbelieving of disbelievers. Rather, their speech is concerning one who has a doubt in what he holds, even if what he holds is in itself disbelief upon investigation, such as the denier of the vision [of Allah] and the punishment of the grave and the like, for it involves denial of well-known texts and consensus, except that they have a doubt of analogy of the unseen on the seen and similar, as known in theology. And like the denier of the caliphate of the two shaykhs and the one who insults them, for it involves denial of definitive consensus, except that they deny the authority of consensus by accusing the Companions, so they have a doubt in general, even if it is apparently invalid with respect to the evidence. So because of that doubt which their ijtihad led to, they are not judged as disbelievers, even though their belief is disbelief, as a precaution, contrary to those like the ghulat we mentioned. So reflect, end. Oppression such as imposing some of the taxes that the Imam has the right to take and causing harm thereby to repel a more general harm. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. He restricted it by their Islam because if dhimmis overcome a place of war, they become people of war as we have stated. However, if the people of baghy seek help from dhimmis and they fight with them, that is not a breach of covenant, just as this action of the people of baghy is not a breach of faith, so their ruling is the ruling of bughat. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Meaning by following the Muslims, so the restriction by Islam is not objected. And by 'Imam' is meant the sultan or his deputy. He said in al-Khaniyya from al-Siyar: Our scholars said: The sultan becomes sultan by two matters: by the pledge of allegiance with him, and consideration is given in the pledge to their nobles and notables; and the second is that his rule be executed over his subjects out of fear of his coercion and domination. If the people pledge allegiance but his rule is not executed over them due to inability to coerce them, he becomes sultan. So if he becomes sultan by pledge and becomes oppressive, if he has coercion and domination, he is not removed, because if he were removed, he would become sultan by coercion and domination, so it is useless. If he does not have coercion and domination, he is removed. End. And he restricted it by their overcoming a land because the ruling of baghy is not established until they overcome, gather, and have strength. Thus in al-Muhit. And the author did not restrict the Imam to being just, but it is restricted in Fath al-Qadir by the condition that the people are safe with him and the roads are safe. His statement: (And he begins by fighting them) meaning when they have fortified and gathered, and this is the choice based on what Khawarizadeh transmitted from our companions that we initiate them before they initiate us, because the ruling is based on the indicator, which is gathering and resistance. This is because if the Imam waited for their actual fighting, perhaps he would not be able to repel, so the ruling is based on the indicator out of necessity to repel their evil. And it is transmitted that he does not initiate them until they initiate him; if they initiate him, he fights them until their group is dispersed. The apparent meaning of their speech is that the first is the madhhab. And in al-Bada'i': It is obligatory upon everyone whom the Imam calls to fight them to respond, and it is not permissible for them to stay behind if they have strength and capability, because obedience to the Imam in what is not disobedience is fard, so how about what is obedience? As for Abu Hanifah's withdrawal in fitna and staying home, it is interpreted as when he is not called. But if the Imam calls him, responding is fard. End. As for some Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, staying behind, it is interpreted as that they did not have ability, and perhaps some of them were in doubt about the permissibility of fighting. And the hadith, 'When two Muslims meet with their swords, both the killer and the killed are in the Fire,' is interpreted as their fighting out of tribal zeal or partisanship, as occurs between people of two villages or two districts, or for worldly reasons and kingdom. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If the people of baghy request a truce, they are responded to if it is better for the Muslims, as with the people of war, and nothing is taken from them. If we take hostages from them and they take hostages from us, then they betray us and kill our hostages, we should not kill their hostages because the hostages have become secure in our hands and the condition of permitting their blood is invalid. Rather, they are imprisoned until the people of baghy perish or repent. Similarly, if the people of shirk do that with our hostages, we do not do it. Al-Quduri.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the definition and rulings regarding rebels (bughat). The author begins by addressing the issue of takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever), distinguishing between what is said by ordinary people and what is established by the jurists. He quotes Ibn al-Mundhir's assertion that no jurist agrees with the hadith scholars in declaring the Khawarij disbelievers, and this is effectively a consensus of the fuqaha. He then defines bughat as Muslims who rebel against the just Imam but do not go as far as the Khawarij in making lawful the blood of Muslims. The text then explains the procedure the Imam should follow: first, call them back and clarify their doubts, asking the reason for their rebellion. If it is due to oppression, the Imam must remove it; if they claim truth is with them, then they are bughat. This is based on the action of Ali ibn Abi Talib with the people of Harura (Khawarij) before fighting them. The author notes that this call is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib, because if the Imam fights without calling, he is not sinning since the rebels are aware of what they are fighting for. The passage then discusses the conditions for being considered bughat: they must have overcome a land and gathered strength. If they rebel due to clear oppression, they are not bughat, and Muslims must help them against the oppressor. The author clarifies that extreme sects like the ghulat of the Rafidah, who hold beliefs like the divinity of Ali, are not given the benefit of the doubt because they have no shubha (doubt) from ijtihad; they are simply following whims. For those with some doubt, like deniers of the vision of Allah or the punishment of the grave, despite their belief being kufr, they are not declared disbelievers due to their shubha. Similarly, those who deny the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar or insult them have a shubha (by accusing the Companions), so they are not declared disbelievers as a precaution. The passage also covers the definition of the Imam or sultan, stating that he becomes sultan by either a pledge of allegiance from the nobles or by having coercive power over the subjects. A sultan who becomes oppressive but has power is not removed, contrary to one without power. Regarding initiating hostilities, the author mentions two views: the preferred view (mukhtar) is that the Imam should initiate fighting if they have gathered, because the indicator of rebellion is their gathering and resistance, and waiting might lead to inability to repel. The other view is that he waits for them to initiate. The passage also discusses the obligation to respond to the Imam's call to fight, and that Abu Hanifah's withdrawal from fitna applies only when not called. Finally, it covers the ruling on hostages: if bughat request a truce, it is granted if beneficial; if hostages are taken and betrayed, we do not kill their hostages but imprison them until the bughat repent or die. This ruling is based on the principle that hostages are under aman (security) and conditions permitting their blood are invalid. The overall teaching is that bughat are Muslims with some mistaken interpretation, so they are not treated as disbelievers, but the Imam must take action to maintain order while minimizing harm.

Key Terms
البغاة (al-bughāt): Muslims who rebel against the just Imam and have overcome a land, but do not make lawful what the Khawarij do (like killing Muslims and taking property). They are distinguished by having a shubha (misinterpretation).
الخوارج (al-khawārij): A group that rebels against the legitimate ruler and deems lawful the blood and wealth of Muslims based on a misinterpretation. They are not declared disbelievers by the Hanafi school if they have a shubha, but their actions are condemned.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): A plausible but incorrect interpretation that leads someone to hold a false belief or action. In this context, it prevents takfir and protects the rebel from being treated as an apostate.
التكفير (al-takfīr): Declaring someone to be a disbeliever (kafir). The text emphasizes that this is a serious matter and should only be done by mujtahid jurists, and even then, bughat are not declared kafir due to shubha.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the Khawarij not declared disbelievers despite making lawful the blood of Muslims?
A) Because they are considered apostates but not kafir.
B) Because they have a ta'wil (misinterpretation) and a shubha.
C) Because they have repented.
D) Because they do not actually kill Muslims.

Key Takeaway
Bughat and even Khawarij are not declared disbelievers as long as they have a plausible misinterpretation (shubha); this is a core Hanafi principle of caution in takfir.

The Imam's Procedure with Bughat: Calling, Initiating Fight, and Hostages
Lays out the Imam's procedure with bughat: calling, initiating, and hostage rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rulings on the Imam's procedure with bughat, including when to call them, when to initiate fighting, and how to handle hostages.

Core Idea
The Imam must first call the bughat and expose their doubt; after that, he should initiate fighting when they have gathered, based on the indicator of rebellion.

Once bughat are identified, the Hanafi school provides a detailed procedure for the Imam. The first step is to call them and ask the reason for their rebellion, following the precedent of Ali ibn Abi Talib with the people of Harura. This call is recommended, not obligatory. Then, the Imam must decide whether to initiate fighting or wait. The preferred view is to initiate when they have gathered, because the indicator of rebellion is their collective strength. Finally, the passage covers rules about hostages and truces, emphasizing that hostages are protected even if the bughat betray.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first step: calling the bughat
The Imam must call the bughat and ask them why they rebelled. If they mention oppression, he removes it. If they claim truth is with them, they are confirmed as bughat.
This is based on Ali's action with the people of Harura (Khawarij).
2
Determine the ruling on the call
The call is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib. If the Imam fights without calling, he is not sinning because the bughat are aware of what they are fighting for.
The text says 'istihbaban la wujuban'.
3
Examine the internal dispute on initiating fight
Two views: (1) The Imam should initiate fighting when they gather (preferred, based on indicators). (2) He should wait for them to strike first (transmitted from some companions). The first view is chosen because waiting may lead to inability to repel evil.
The ruling is based on the indicator (dilalah) of gathering and resistance.
4
Apply the hostage ruling
If bughat request a truce, it is granted if beneficial. If hostages are taken and the bughat betray by killing the hostages, we do not kill their hostages in retaliation because the hostages are under aman. Instead, they are imprisoned until the bughat repent or perish.
This is analogous to the ruling with people of war, but with a key difference: we do not kill hostages even if they kill ours.

Book of Siyar, Chapter of Bughat. Ibn al-Mundhir said: I know no one who agreed with the hadith scholars on declaring them disbelievers, and this implies transmission of consensus of the jurists. It is mentioned in al-Muhit that some jurists do not declare anyone from the people of innovation a disbeliever, and some declare some of them disbelievers, which is one who opposed with his innovation a definitive proof, and he attributed it to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, and the first report is more established. Indeed, much takfir occurs in the speech of the people of the schools, but it is not from the speech of the jurists who are the mujtahids, but from others, and non-jurists are not considered. And what is transmitted from the mujtahids is what we mentioned, and Ibn al-Mundhir is most knowledgeable in transmitting the schools of the mujtahids. And what Muhammad ibn al-Hasan mentioned from the hadith of al-Hadrami indicates the non-takfir of the Khawarij. As for the bughat, they are a group of Muslims who rebelled against the just Imam and did not deem lawful what the Khawarij deemed lawful of the blood of Muslims and their offspring. So what is in al-Bada'i' from interpreting bughat as Khawarij is deficient. Indeed, we do not declare the Khawarij disbelievers for deeming blood and wealth lawful due to their misinterpretation, even if it is false, contrary to one who deems lawful without interpretation. His statement: (A group of Muslims left obedience to the Imam and overcame a land; he calls them and exposes their doubt by asking them the reason for their rebellion. If it is due to oppression from him, he removes it. If they say, 'The truth is with us and the leadership is ours,' then they are bughat, because Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, did that with the people of Harura before fighting them, and because it is the easier of the two matters, and perhaps evil will be repelled by it. So he begins with it as recommended, not obligatory. For if the people of justice fought them without calling them to return to the community, there would be nothing upon them, because they know what they are fighting for, so their case is like apostates and people of war after the invitation has reached them. Thus in al-Inayah. If they present what permits them to fight, such as if he oppressed them or oppressed others with an oppression having no doubt, then they are not bughat, and it is not permissible to help the Imam against them. Rather, it is obligatory upon the Muslims to help them until he treats them justly and returns from his injustice, contrary to when the case is ambiguous that he sought the truth. But their certainty of the invalidity of prayer behind them does not validate this combination, unless by 'non-permissibility' is meant non-permissibility with validity, otherwise it is problematic. Thus mentioned by Shaykh Kamal al-Din ibn al-Humam. Upon this, what is transmitted should be interpreted as excluding the ghulat of the Rafidah and those similar to them, for their like have not exerted effort in ijtihad. Indeed, one who says that Ali is God or that Jibril made a mistake and the like of such nonsense is only following the worst case: pure whim, and is among those who said, 'We only worship them that they may bring us near to Allah.' So it is not conceivable from the two great Imams that they would not rule that they are among the most disbelieving of disbelievers. Rather, their speech is concerning one who has a doubt in what he holds, even if what he holds is in itself disbelief upon investigation, such as the denier of the vision [of Allah] and the punishment of the grave and the like, for it involves denial of well-known texts and consensus, except that they have a doubt of analogy of the unseen on the seen and similar, as known in theology. And like the denier of the caliphate of the two shaykhs and the one who insults them, for it involves denial of definitive consensus, except that they deny the authority of consensus by accusing the Companions, so they have a doubt in general, even if it is apparently invalid with respect to the evidence. So because of that doubt which their ijtihad led to, they are not judged as disbelievers, even though their belief is disbelief, as a precaution, contrary to those like the ghulat we mentioned. So reflect, end. Oppression such as imposing some of the taxes that the Imam has the right to take and causing harm thereby to repel a more general harm. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. He restricted it by their Islam because if dhimmis overcome a place of war, they become people of war as we have stated. However, if the people of baghy seek help from dhimmis and they fight with them, that is not a breach of covenant, just as this action of the people of baghy is not a breach of faith, so their ruling is the ruling of bughat. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Meaning by following the Muslims, so the restriction by Islam is not objected. And by 'Imam' is meant the sultan or his deputy. He said in al-Khaniyya from al-Siyar: Our scholars said: The sultan becomes sultan by two matters: by the pledge of allegiance with him, and consideration is given in the pledge to their nobles and notables; and the second is that his rule be executed over his subjects out of fear of his coercion and domination. If the people pledge allegiance but his rule is not executed over them due to inability to coerce them, he becomes sultan. So if he becomes sultan by pledge and becomes oppressive, if he has coercion and domination, he is not removed, because if he were removed, he would become sultan by coercion and domination, so it is useless. If he does not have coercion and domination, he is removed. End. And he restricted it by their overcoming a land because the ruling of baghy is not established until they overcome, gather, and have strength. Thus in al-Muhit. And the author did not restrict the Imam to being just, but it is restricted in Fath al-Qadir by the condition that the people are safe with him and the roads are safe. His statement: (And he begins by fighting them) meaning when they have fortified and gathered, and this is the choice based on what Khawarizadeh transmitted from our companions that we initiate them before they initiate us, because the ruling is based on the indicator, which is gathering and resistance. This is because if the Imam waited for their actual fighting, perhaps he would not be able to repel, so the ruling is based on the indicator out of necessity to repel their evil. And it is transmitted that he does not initiate them until they initiate him; if they initiate him, he fights them until their group is dispersed. The apparent meaning of their speech is that the first is the madhhab. And in al-Bada'i': It is obligatory upon everyone whom the Imam calls to fight them to respond, and it is not permissible for them to stay behind if they have strength and capability, because obedience to the Imam in what is not disobedience is fard, so how about what is obedience? As for Abu Hanifah's withdrawal in fitna and staying home, it is interpreted as when he is not called. But if the Imam calls him, responding is fard. End. As for some Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, staying behind, it is interpreted as that they did not have ability, and perhaps some of them were in doubt about the permissibility of fighting. And the hadith, 'When two Muslims meet with their swords, both the killer and the killed are in the Fire,' is interpreted as their fighting out of tribal zeal or partisanship, as occurs between people of two villages or two districts, or for worldly reasons and kingdom. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Muhit: If the people of baghy request a truce, they are responded to if it is better for the Muslims, as with the people of war, and nothing is taken from them. If we take hostages from them and they take hostages from us, then they betray us and kill our hostages, we should not kill their hostages because the hostages have become secure in our hands and the condition of permitting their blood is invalid. Rather, they are imprisoned until the people of baghy perish or repent. Similarly, if the people of shirk do that with our hostages, we do not do it. Al-Quduri.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the definition and rulings regarding rebels (bughat). The author begins by addressing the issue of takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever), distinguishing between what is said by ordinary people and what is established by the jurists. He quotes Ibn al-Mundhir's assertion that no jurist agrees with the hadith scholars in declaring the Khawarij disbelievers, and this is effectively a consensus of the fuqaha. He then defines bughat as Muslims who rebel against the just Imam but do not go as far as the Khawarij in making lawful the blood of Muslims. The text then explains the procedure the Imam should follow: first, call them back and clarify their doubts, asking the reason for their rebellion. If it is due to oppression, the Imam must remove it; if they claim truth is with them, then they are bughat. This is based on the action of Ali ibn Abi Talib with the people of Harura (Khawarij) before fighting them. The author notes that this call is mustahabb (recommended), not wajib, because if the Imam fights without calling, he is not sinning since the rebels are aware of what they are fighting for. The passage then discusses the conditions for being considered bughat: they must have overcome a land and gathered strength. If they rebel due to clear oppression, they are not bughat, and Muslims must help them against the oppressor. The author clarifies that extreme sects like the ghulat of the Rafidah, who hold beliefs like the divinity of Ali, are not given the benefit of the doubt because they have no shubha (doubt) from ijtihad; they are simply following whims. For those with some doubt, like deniers of the vision of Allah or the punishment of the grave, despite their belief being kufr, they are not declared disbelievers due to their shubha. Similarly, those who deny the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar or insult them have a shubha (by accusing the Companions), so they are not declared disbelievers as a precaution. The passage also covers the definition of the Imam or sultan, stating that he becomes sultan by either a pledge of allegiance from the nobles or by having coercive power over the subjects. A sultan who becomes oppressive but has power is not removed, contrary to one without power. Regarding initiating hostilities, the author mentions two views: the preferred view (mukhtar) is that the Imam should initiate fighting if they have gathered, because the indicator of rebellion is their gathering and resistance, and waiting might lead to inability to repel. The other view is that he waits for them to initiate. The passage also discusses the obligation to respond to the Imam's call to fight, and that Abu Hanifah's withdrawal from fitna applies only when not called. Finally, it covers the ruling on hostages: if bughat request a truce, it is granted if beneficial; if hostages are taken and betrayed, we do not kill their hostages but imprison them until the bughat repent or die. This ruling is based on the principle that hostages are under aman (security) and conditions permitting their blood are invalid. The overall teaching is that bughat are Muslims with some mistaken interpretation, so they are not treated as disbelievers, but the Imam must take action to maintain order while minimizing harm.

Key Terms
المبايعة (al-mubāyaʿah): The pledge of allegiance given to a ruler. In the context of this passage, it is one way a person becomes sultan (ruler).
الامتناع (al-imtināʿ): Refusal or resistance. In this passage, it is the indicator that the bughat have rebelled and are ready to fight.
الموادعة (al-muwādaʿah): Truce or peace agreement. The Imam may grant a truce to bughat if it is beneficial to the Muslims.
الرهون (al-ruhūn): Hostages taken as security. The passage discusses rules of dealing with hostages from bughat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should the Imam do if the bughat request a truce?
A) Reject it outright because they are rebels.
B) Accept it only if they disarm completely.
C) Accept it if it is better for the Muslims, and take nothing from them.
D) Accept it only if they pay a ransom.

Key Takeaway
The Imam should call bughat first (recommended), then initiate fighting when they gather (preferred view), and hostages are under aman and cannot be killed in retaliation.

How a Sultan Becomes Legitimate and When He Is Removed
Defines how a person becomes sultan and when he is removed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two ways a person becomes a sultan according to the Hanafi school and explain the condition for his removal.

Core Idea
A sultan is legitimate through bay'ah or effective domination; even an unjust sultan is not removed if he still has power.

This passage from al-Khaniyah defines the legal foundation of the sultan's authority. You will learn that the sultanate can be established by a formal pledge or by effective control, and that the school's pragmatic approach prevents removal of a strong ruler even if unjust, to avoid greater harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases of sultanate
Read the text and extract the two matters by which a person becomes a sultan: mubaya'ah (bay'ah) and qahr/ghalabah (domination).
These are the only two sources of legitimacy in this view.
2
Understand the role of bay'ah
Note that bay'ah must come from the nobles and prominent people (ashraf wa a'yan), not the entire populace. If bay'ah is given but the ruler cannot enforce his rule, he does not become sultan.
Effectiveness is key even after bay'ah.
3
Understand the role of effective domination
If the ruler enforces his commands through fear and power, he is sultan even without bay'ah. This is a de facto legitimacy.
The school prioritizes stability.
4
Analyse the ruling on an unjust sultan
Once sultan through bay'ah, if he becomes unjust but still holds power, he is not removed. The reason: removal would be futile as he would regain power by force. If he lacks power, he is removed.
This is a unique Hanafi concession.
5
Apply to the bughat context
This definition determines who is the legitimate imam against whom rebellion is prohibited. An unjust but powerful sultan remains the authority.
Relevance to the chapter on rebels.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الخانية من السير قال علماؤنا : السلطان من يصير سلطاناً بأمرين : بالمبايعة معه ويعتبر في المبايعة أشرافهم وأعيانهم، والثاني أن ينفذ حكمه في رعيته خوفاً من قهره وجبروته، فإن بايع الناس ولم ينفذ حكمه فيهم لعجزه عن قهرهم يصير سلطاناً، فإذا صار سلطاناً بالمبايعة فجار إن كان له قهر وغلبة لا ينعزل لأنه لو انعزل سلطاناً يصير بالقهر والغلبة فلا يفيد، وإن لم يكن له قهر وغلبة ينعزل .اهـ.
It is stated in al-Khaniyah from the book of Siyar: Our scholars said: The sultan becomes a sultan by two matters: by the pledge of allegiance given to him, and the nobles and prominent people are considered in the pledge; and the second is that his command be executed among his subjects out of fear of his domination and compulsion. If the people gave him the pledge and he does not execute his command among them due to his inability to dominate them, he becomes a sultan. When he becomes a sultan by the pledge, if he is unjust but has domination and superiority, he is not removed, because if he is removed, he would become a sultan by domination and superiority, so it is of no benefit. And if he has no domination and superiority, he is removed. End.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the legitimacy of a ruler (sultan) in the context of fighting rebels. The scholars state that a person becomes a sultan through one of two ways: first, by receiving a pledge of allegiance (bay'ah) from the elite and leaders of the community; second, by effectively enforcing his rule over his subjects through fear of his power and compulsion. If the people give bay'ah but the ruler cannot enforce his commands due to weakness, he does not become a sultan. However, once he becomes a sultan through bay'ah, even if he becomes unjust (jar), he is not removed from power if he still has the strength and domination to enforce his rule. The reasoning is that removing him would only lead to him regaining power through force, thus defeating the purpose. Conversely, if he lacks the power to enforce his rule, he is removed. This ruling is significant for the chapter on rebels because it determines who is the legitimate imam whose authority must be obeyed in fighting the bughat.

Key Terms
السلطان (al-sultān): The ruler or sovereign who holds authority over the community; in this context, the imam or his deputy authorized to lead the fight against rebels.
المبايعة (al-mubāya‘ah): The pledge of allegiance given by the nobles and prominent people to the sultan, establishing his legitimacy.
القهر والغلبة (al-qahr wa al-ghalabah): Domination and superiority through force, which also establishes the sultan's effective authority even without bay'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars in al-Khaniyah, what is one way a person becomes a sultan?
A) By being elected by all Muslims
B) By receiving a pledge of allegiance from the nobles and leaders
C) By being appointed by the previous sultan
D) By being the oldest male in the ruling family

Key Takeaway
A sultan is legitimate through bay'ah or effective domination; an unjust sultan is not removed as long as he holds power, to avoid greater evil.

Initiating War Against Rebels: The Hanafi Dispute
Resolving an intra-Hanafi debate on the timing of war against rebels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi opinions on initiating combat against rebels, explain the preferred view's evidence, and apply the distinction to the hadith about the killer and killed.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school prefers to initiate war against rebels as soon as they gather, not waiting for their attack.

This lesson examines a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding when the imam should engage rebels (bughat). The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq cites a preferred view that the imam should initiate fighting upon the rebels' gathering and resistance, and an opposing view that he should wait for them to strike first. Understanding this dispute clarifies the broader legal principles governing rebellion in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage to find the two opinions: one that the imam should initiate fighting when rebels gather, and another that he should wait for them to start fighting.
2
Analyze the evidence for the preferred view
The preferred view bases the ruling on the rebels' gathering and resistance (al-ijtima' wa al-imtina'), not on their actual attack. Why does the text argue that waiting might be ineffective?
The text says 'maybe he won't be able to repel them' if he waits for their attack.
3
Respond to the opposing view
The text mentions a narration that the imam should not initiate. What is the Hanafi response to the hadith about the killer and the killed?
The hadith is restricted to fights based on tribal zeal or worldly reasons, not legitimate authority.
4
Determine the obligation to respond to the imam's call
According to al-Bada'i', responding to the imam's call to fight rebels is fard. How is the narration from Abu Hanifa about withdrawing during fitnah reconciled?
Abu Hanifa's advice applies only when the imam has not summoned; if called, it's fard.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
If a rebel group with a valid ta'wil gathers in a city and refuses to obey the imam, what should the imam do according to the preferred view?
He should initiate action to disperse them before they attack.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبدأ بقتالهم) يعني إذا تعسكروا واجتمعوا وهو اختيار لما نقله خواهر زاده عن أصحابنا أنا نبدؤهم قبل أن يبدؤنا لأن الحكم يدار على الدليل وهو الاجتماع والامتناع. وهذا لأنه لو انتظر الإمام حقيقة قتالهم ربما لا يمكنه الدفع فيدار على الدليل ضرورة دفع شرهم. ونقل أنه لا يبدؤهم حتى يبدؤه فإن بدؤه قاتلهم حتى يفرق جمعهم، وظاهر كلامهم أن المذهب الأول. وفي البدائع : يجب على كل من دعاهم الإمام إلى قتالهم أن يجيب ولا يسعهم التخلف إذا كان له غنى وقدرة لأن طاعة الإمام فيما ليس بمعصية فرض فكيف فيما هو طاعة؟ وما عن أبي حنيفة من الاعتزال في الفتنة ولزوم البيت محمول على ما إذا لم يدعه أما إذا أدعاه الإمام فالإجابة فرض .اهـ. وأما تخلف بعض الصحابة رضي الله عنهم فمحمول على أنه لم يكن لهم قدرة وربما كان بعضهم في تردد من حل القتال، وما روي «إذا التقى المؤمنان بسيوفهما فالقاتل والمقتول في النار» محمول على اقتتالهما حمية وعصبة كما يتفق بين أهل قريتين أو محلتين أو لأجل الدنيا والمملكة؛ كذا في فتح القدير.
His statement: (and he begins fighting them) meaning when they become entrenched and gather. This is a preference based on what Khwaharzadeh narrated from our companions: that we initiate fighting them before they initiate fighting us, because the ruling is based on the evidence, which is their gathering and resistance. And this is because if the imam waits for their actual fighting, perhaps he will not be able to repel them, so the ruling is based on the evidence out of necessity to repel their evil. And it was narrated that he does not initiate fighting until they initiate; if they initiate, he fights them until he disperses their gathering. And the apparent meaning of their words is that the first opinion is the madhhab. In al-Bada'i': It is obligatory (wajib) for everyone whom the imam calls to fight them to respond, and it is not permissible to refrain if one has the means and ability, because obedience to the imam in what is not disobedience is fard, so how about in what is obedience? And what is narrated from Abu Hanifah about withdrawing in times of fitna and staying home is interpreted as when the imam has not called him; but if the imam calls him, then response is fard. End. As for the delay of some companions, may Allah be pleased with them, it is interpreted as their lack of ability, and perhaps some of them were in doubt about the permissibility of fighting. And the hadith 'When two believers meet with their swords, both the killer and the killed are in the Fire' is interpreted as referring to fighting out of tribal zeal and partisanship, such as occurs between two villages or two districts, or for worldly purposes or dominion; this is from Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding initiating war against rebels (bughat). The author of al-Hidayah (the matn commentator) states the preferred view: the imam begins fighting the rebels when they gather and become entrenched, before they actually attack. This is based on the principle that the ruling is tied to the evidence (their gathering), and waiting for them to attack might leave the imam unable to repel the harm. There is a transmitted opinion that the imam should not initiate until they do, but the author notes that the apparent position of the scholars is the first view. The passage then cites al-Bada'i' to emphasize that responding to the imam's call to fight the rebels is obligatory (fard), not just recommended; the famous narration from Abu Hanifah about withdrawing during fitna applies only when the imam has not summoned the individual. Finally, the hadith threatening the killer and killed with hellfire is restricted to cases of tribal or worldly fighting, not fighting against rebels under the imam's authority. This ruling ensures that the legitimate authority is protected and rebellion is suppressed efficiently.

Key Terms
البغاة (al-bughāt): Rebels: Muslims who revolt against the legitimate imam and have a valid interpretation (ta'wil) for their rebellion.
الفئة (al-fi’ah): A group or party; in this context, a band of rebels with organization and leadership.
الفتنة (al-fitnah): Civil strife or communal conflict; Abu Hanifah advised staying away from fitnah if the imam has not called for action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, when should the imam begin fighting rebels?
A) Only after the rebels have killed a Muslim
B) When the rebels become entrenched and gather, before they attack
C) Only after three warnings have been given
D) Only if the rebels are non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers proactive engagement against rebels based on their gathering, not on their actual attack, and restricts the hadith warning to personal or tribal violence.

The Fi'ah Condition for Pursuit and Finishing Off Wounded Rebels
Ruling on pursuit and finishing off wounded conditioned on rebel faction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a just imam may finish off wounded rebels and pursue fugitives, and apply the ruling on prisoner discretion.

Core Idea
If rebels have a faction, finish them; if not, spare them – the threat determines the measure.

In the context of fighting rebels (bughat), the Hanafi school distinguishes between a defeated group with no remaining leadership and one that still has an organized faction. This lesson explains why that distinction matters for wounding, pursuit, and imprisonment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
The act is finishing off wounded rebels and pursuing their fugitives. The condition is the existence of a rebel faction (fi'ah) to which they can return.
The condition is explicitly stated in the text: 'if they have a faction'.
2
Understand the rationale
If a faction exists, the rebels might regroup and resume fighting, so severe measures are needed to repel their evil. If not, the evil is already repelled by their defeat.
The phrase 'li daf'i sharrihim' indicates the reason.
3
Note the prisoner ruling
The imam has discretion to kill or imprison a captured rebel, also based on the need to repel evil. This is from al-Bada'i'.
No mention of a condition for prisoners – the imam chooses.
4
Recognize the distinction from non-Muslim combatants
The same reasoning applies but with differences in treatment of non-combatants and mahram, which will be covered in another lesson.
This lesson focuses on the fi'ah condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم واتبع موليهم وإلا (لا) أي وإن لم يكن لهم فئة لا يجهز على الجريح ولا يتبع المولى لدفع شرهم بالأول كيلا يلحقوا بهم ولاندفاع الشر دونه في الثاني، والفئة الطائفة والجمع فئون وفئات وجهز على الجريح كمنع وأجهز ثبت قتله وأسرعه وتمم عليه وموت مجهز وجهيز سريع؛ كذا في القاموس. «واتبع» على البناء للمفعول للقتل والأسر وموليهم بالنصب مفعول ثانٍ وهو اسم فاعل من ولي تولية أدبر كتولى ولم يذكر حكم أسيرهم. وفي البدائع : إن شاء الإمام قتله وإن شاء حبسه لاندفاع شره به. ويقاتل أهل البغي بالمنجنيق والغرق وغير ذلك كأهل الحرب. وكل من لا يجوز قتله من أهل الحرب من النساء والصبيان والشيوخ والعميان لا يجوز قتله من أهل البغي إلا إذا قاتلوا فيقتلون حال القتال وبعد الفراغ إلا الصبيان والمجانين. ولا يجوز للعادل أن يبتدئ بقتل محرمه من أهل البغي مباشرة إلا إذا أراد قتله فله أن يدفعه ولو بقتله، وله أن يتسبب ليقتله غيره كعقر دابته بخلاف أهل الحرب فإن له أن يقتل محرمه منهم مباشرة إلا الوالدين اهـ.
His statement: (and if they have a faction, he finishes off their wounded and pursues their fugitives; otherwise, not) i.e., if they have no faction, he does not finish off the wounded nor pursue the fugitive, to repel their evil by the first so that they do not rejoin them, and because repelling evil is less needed in the second. The word al-fi'ah means group, its plurals are fu'un and fi'at. The phrase 'ajhaza 'ala al-jarih' means he killed him and hastened his death, completed it; and 'majhaz' and 'jihiz' mean swift. This is from al-Qamus. 'Wa utbi'a' is passive, meaning he is pursued for killing and capture; 'mawlihim' is in the accusative as a second object, and it is an active participle from wala, meaning to turn away. And he did not mention the ruling for their prisoner. In al-Bada'i': The imam may kill him or imprison him to repel his evil. And one fights the rebels with catapults, flooding, and other means, just as with the people of war. And every category of people from the people of war whose killing is not allowed—women, children, the elderly, and the blind—their killing is also not allowed from the rebels, except if they fight, then they are killed during the fighting and after it is finished, except for children and the insane. And it is not permissible for a just fighter to initiate the killing of his own relative from the rebels directly, unless the relative intends to kill him; then he may defend himself even by killing him. And he may cause someone else to kill him, such as by hamstringing his animal—contrary to the people of war, where he may kill his relative from them directly, except for parents. End.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on the treatment of rebels. It explains the condition for finishing off wounded rebels and pursuing those who flee: this is only permissible if the rebels still have an organized faction (fi'ah) to which they could return and resume fighting. If they no longer have a centralized leadership, then the objective is achieved by their defeat, so there is no need to kill wounded or capture fugitives. The text then defines key terms. It notes that al-Bada'i' gives the imam discretion over prisoners: he may kill them or imprison them. The rules of engagement are similar to those against enemy non-Muslims (ahl al-harb), but with some differences: civilians (women, children, elderly, blind) are not to be killed unless they actively fight, except children and the insane are never killed. A notable difference from fighting against ahl al-harb is that a just fighter may not intentionally kill his own blood relative (mahram) from the rebels directly unless the relative attacks him; but with ahl al-harb, he may kill his mahram except for parents. This shows the Hanafi sensitivity to kinship ties and the hope of the rebel's return to obedience.

Key Terms
الفئة (al-fi’ah): An organized group of rebels with leadership and strength; the presence of a fi'ah determines whether to pursue fugitives and kill wounded.
أجهز على الجريح (ajhaza 'ala al-jarīh): To finish off a wounded person, i.e., to kill him swiftly and put him out of misery; this is permitted if the rebels have a fi'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the imam finish off wounded rebels and pursue their fugitives?
A) In all cases, to prevent future rebellion
B) Only if the rebels have a faction (fi'ah) to which they could return
C) Only after the rebels kill a Muslim
D) Only for leaders, not for ordinary fighters

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of harsh measures against rebels depends on whether they still have an organized faction to return to.

Non-Combatant Immunity and Kinship Rules in Fighting Rebels
Comparison of non-combatant and mahram killing in rebel vs. ahl al-harb contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categories of rebels are immune from killing and apply the distinction between killing a rebel mahram and a mahram from ahl al-harb.

Core Idea
Rebel non-combatants are protected unless they fight; killing a rebel mahram requires self-defense.

While rebels are fought with similar methods as non-Muslim enemies, the Hanafi school imposes additional restrictions to protect vulnerable categories and preserve family ties. This lesson compares the treatment of non-combatants and blood relatives in rebel conflicts with that in wars against ahl al-harb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the protected categories
Women, children, the elderly, and the blind are not to be killed unless they actively fight. Children and the insane are never killed even if they fight.
The text says 'except if they fight, then they are killed... except for children and the insane.'
2
Understand the comparison with ahl al-harb
The protection for these categories is the same as in war against non-Muslims. However, the exception for fighting is more restrictive for rebels: if they fight, they can be killed during and after the battle, but children and insane are always exempt.
For ahl al-harb, killing non-combatants is also prohibited, but the exception for fighting is similar.
3
Analyze the mahram ruling
A just fighter may not initiate the killing of his own mahram (close relative) from the rebels directly. He may only kill if the mahram attacks him. He may cause someone else to kill the mahram indirectly (e.g., by hamstringing his horse).
Contrast with ahl al-harb: killing mahram is allowed directly, except for parents.
4
Apply the rules to scenarios
Given a situation with a rebel group containing women and children, determine who may be targeted during and after battle.
Focus on the restrictions and exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو لهم فئة أجهز على جريحهم واتبع موليهم وإلا (لا) أي وإن لم يكن لهم فئة لا يجهز على الجريح ولا يتبع المولى لدفع شرهم بالأول كيلا يلحقوا بهم ولاندفاع الشر دونه في الثاني، والفئة الطائفة والجمع فئون وفئات وجهز على الجريح كمنع وأجهز ثبت قتله وأسرعه وتمم عليه وموت مجهز وجهيز سريع؛ كذا في القاموس. «واتبع» على البناء للمفعول للقتل والأسر وموليهم بالنصب مفعول ثانٍ وهو اسم فاعل من ولي تولية أدبر كتولى ولم يذكر حكم أسيرهم. وفي البدائع : إن شاء الإمام قتله وإن شاء حبسه لاندفاع شره به. ويقاتل أهل البغي بالمنجنيق والغرق وغير ذلك كأهل الحرب. وكل من لا يجوز قتله من أهل الحرب من النساء والصبيان والشيوخ والعميان لا يجوز قتله من أهل البغي إلا إذا قاتلوا فيقتلون حال القتال وبعد الفراغ إلا الصبيان والمجانين. ولا يجوز للعادل أن يبتدئ بقتل محرمه من أهل البغي مباشرة إلا إذا أراد قتله فله أن يدفعه ولو بقتله، وله أن يتسبب ليقتله غيره كعقر دابته بخلاف أهل الحرب فإن له أن يقتل محرمه منهم مباشرة إلا الوالدين اهـ.
His statement: (and if they have a faction, he finishes off their wounded and pursues their fugitives; otherwise, not) i.e., if they have no faction, he does not finish off the wounded nor pursue the fugitive, to repel their evil by the first so that they do not rejoin them, and because repelling evil is less needed in the second. The word al-fi'ah means group, its plurals are fu'un and fi'at. The phrase 'ajhaza 'ala al-jarih' means he killed him and hastened his death, completed it; and 'majhaz' and 'jihiz' mean swift. This is from al-Qamus. 'Wa utbi'a' is passive, meaning he is pursued for killing and capture; 'mawlihim' is in the accusative as a second object, and it is an active participle from wala, meaning to turn away. And he did not mention the ruling for their prisoner. In al-Bada'i': The imam may kill him or imprison him to repel his evil. And one fights the rebels with catapults, flooding, and other means, just as with the people of war. And every category of people from the people of war whose killing is not allowed—women, children, the elderly, and the blind—their killing is also not allowed from the rebels, except if they fight, then they are killed during the fighting and after it is finished, except for children and the insane. And it is not permissible for a just fighter to initiate the killing of his own relative from the rebels directly, unless the relative intends to kill him; then he may defend himself even by killing him. And he may cause someone else to kill him, such as by hamstringing his animal—contrary to the people of war, where he may kill his relative from them directly, except for parents. End.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on the treatment of rebels. It explains the condition for finishing off wounded rebels and pursuing those who flee: this is only permissible if the rebels still have an organized faction (fi'ah) to which they could return and resume fighting. If they no longer have a centralized leadership, then the objective is achieved by their defeat, so there is no need to kill wounded or capture fugitives. The text then defines key terms. It notes that al-Bada'i' gives the imam discretion over prisoners: he may kill them or imprison them. The rules of engagement are similar to those against enemy non-Muslims (ahl al-harb), but with some differences: civilians (women, children, elderly, blind) are not to be killed unless they actively fight, except children and the insane are never killed. A notable difference from fighting against ahl al-harb is that a just fighter may not intentionally kill his own blood relative (mahram) from the rebels directly unless the relative attacks him; but with ahl al-harb, he may kill his mahram except for parents. This shows the Hanafi sensitivity to kinship ties and the hope of the rebel's return to obedience.

Key Terms
المحرم (al-mahram): A close blood relative whom one cannot marry; the ruling forbids a just fighter from directly killing his mahram from the rebels unless attacked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition may a just fighter kill his mahram (close relative) from the rebels?
A) At any time, because they are rebels.
B) Only when the mahram attacks him.
C) Only after the battle ends.
D) Never, even in self-defense.

Key Takeaway
Rebel non-combatants and mahram are protected from direct targeting, but protection is removed if they actively fight, except for children and the insane.

Prohibition of Capturing Rebels' Children and Handling Their Wealth
Rules on captives, slaves, horses, and repentance of rebels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on rebels' children, property, and repentance, and identify the proof from Caliph Ali's practice.

Core Idea
Rebels' children are never enslaved; their horses are sold and price held; weapons can be used in need; repentance grants full immunity.

This lesson examines the unique Hanafi rules for dealing with rebels (baghy) and their dependents. Unlike regular enemies, rebels are still members of the Muslim polity, so their families and property are treated with restraint, aiming at their eventual return. The rulings are derived from the exemplary conduct of Caliph Ali (RA) during the Battle of the Camel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core rulings
State the main rules: (1) children are not taken captive, (2) property is detained until repentance, but horses are sold and price held, (3) the imam may use weapons and horses in need, (4) repentance grants immunity.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the directive of Ali (RA): 'No prisoner is killed, no veil is lifted, no property is taken.' This establishes the prohibition of enslaving women and children and the protection of property.
Ali's practice is the authoritative precedent for the Hanafi school.
3
Understand the interpretations and exceptions
The text clarifies that the prohibition on killing prisoners applies when rebels have no faction. Slaves are treated differently: those who fought can be killed, others are detained. Horses are sold, not detained in kind, to benefit the owner.
These exceptions show the balance between punishment and hope of return.
4
Note the rationale and principle
The imam may use weapons and horses in battle due to the principle of 'inflicting lesser harm to repel greater harm'. The overall aim is to bring rebels back to compliance.
This principle (al-darar al-ashadd yuzal bi al-darar al-akhaff) governs the use of rebel property.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
Consider how these classical rulings apply to contemporary rebel groups: children and non-combatants are not to be taken as captives; property is not confiscated; the state may use weapons in self-defense but must return value.
Modern military rules of engagement often reflect similar restraint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولم تسب ذريتهم وحبس أموالهم حتى يتوبوا القول علي رضي الله عنه يوم الجمل : ولا يقتل أسير ولا يكشف ستر ولا يؤخذ مال وهو القدوة في هذا الباب. وقوله في لا الأسير مؤول بما إذا لم يكن لهم فئة . ومعنى لا يكشف لهم ستر تسبى نساؤهم. أطلق المال فشمل العبيد فلذا قال في البدائع : وأما العبد المأسور من أهل البغي فإن كان قاتل مع مولاه يجوز قتله، وإن كان يخدم مولاه لا يجوز قتله ولكن يحبس حتى يتوب اهـ. وظاهر ما الكتاب حبس عين الكراع وليس كذلك لما في الهداية وأما الكراع فلا يمسك ولكنه يباع ويحبس ثمنه لمالكه لأنه أنفع له وذكر في المحيط الدواب بدل الكراع . وفي فتح القدير : ولا ينفق عليه من بيت المال لتتوفر مؤنتها عليه وهذا إذا لم يكن للإمام بها حاجة اهـ. قوله : (وإن احتاج قاتل بسلاحهم وخيلهم لأن علياً رضي الله عنه قسم السلاح فيما بين أصحابه بالبصرة وكانت قسمته للحاجة لا للتمليك، ولأن للإمام أن يفعل ذلك في مال العادل عند الحاجة ففي مال الباغي أولى والمعنى فيه إلحاق الضرر الأدنى لدفع الأعلى. قيد بالسلاح والخيل لأن غيرهما من الأموال لا ينتفع به مطلقاً؛ كذا في البدائع. وفي المحيط : قال الباغي تبت وألقى السلاح كف عنه لأن توبة الباغي بمنزلة الإسلام من الحربي في إفادة العصمة
His statement: (And their children are not taken captive, and their wealth is detained until they repent.) The saying of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, on the Day of the Camel: 'No prisoner is killed, no veil is lifted, and no property is taken,' and he is the exemplar in this chapter. His statement about 'no prisoner' is interpreted as when they have no faction. And the meaning of 'no veil is lifted' is that their women are not taken captive. The term 'wealth' is general and includes slaves; therefore al-Bada'i' said: As for the captured slave from the rebels, if he fought alongside his master, it is permissible to kill him; but if he served his master, it is not permissible to kill him; rather, he is detained until he repents. End. The apparent meaning of the book is that the horses (kurā') themselves are detained, but it is not so; according to al-Hidayah, the horses are not held; rather, they are sold and their price is held for their owner, because that is more beneficial for him. And in al-Muhit, 'dawābb' (beasts) is mentioned instead of 'kurā'. In Fath al-Qadir: And no expenditure is made on them from the treasury, so that the owner bears the cost, and this is when the imam has no need of them. End. His statement: (And if he needs, he may fight with their weapons and horses.) Because Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, distributed the weapons among his companions in Basra, and his distribution was based on need, not for ownership. And because the imam may do that with the property of a just person in times of need, so with the property of a rebel it is more appropriate. The rationale is to inflict a lesser harm to repel a greater harm. It is restricted to weapons and horses because other property cannot be used outright, as in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Muhit: If the rebel says 'I repent' and throws down his weapon, he is left alone, because the repentance of a rebel is like the acceptance of Islam from a harbi in granting protection.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof covering several aspects of handling rebels' dependents and property. The foundational ruling is that rebels' children are not enslaved (saby is prohibited), and their property is detained until they repent. This is based on the practice of Caliph Ali (RA) at the Battle of the Camel. The prohibition on killing prisoners and exposing women is interpreted conditionally: if the rebels have no faction, prisoners are spared. Slaves are treated differently: those who fought are killed, while those who merely served are detained. As for property, the author clarifies that horses (kurā') are not held in kind; they are sold and the proceeds held for their owner, which is more beneficial. The imam can use the rebels' weapons and horses if needed for battle, because Ali did so, and the principle of repelling greater harm by inflicting lesser harm applies. Finally, if a rebel repents and throws down his weapons, he is left alone, as repentance is like conversion to Islam for a harbi. These rules aim to balance the need for order with mercy and the hope of return.

Key Terms
الذريّة (al-dhurriyyah): The offspring of the rebels; prohibited from being taken as captives (saby).
الكراع (al-kurā‘): Horses or cavalry equipment (horses with their gear); specifically, the text says it is sold and the price held for the owner.
التوبة (al-tawbah): Repentance of the rebel: by laying down arms and denouncing rebellion, it grants them immunity, similar to a harbi's acceptance of Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling regarding the horses (kurā') of the rebels according to the text?
A) They are confiscated and added to the war booty
B) They are detained in kind until the rebels repent
C) They are sold and the price is held for their owner
D) They are returned immediately after the battle

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law treats rebels as wayward Muslims, not enemies, so their dependents and property are protected with the aim of repentance and return.
