Establishing Lineage of the Foundling by Istihsan
Ruling on accepting lineage claims for the foundling, including from non-finders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts lineage claims for a foundling from any claimant by istihsan, overriding qiyas, and distinguish the treatment of claims by the finder vs. others.

Core Idea
A foundling's lineage is established from any single claimant by istihsan, because the child's need for honor outweighs the finder's right.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on establishing the lineage (nasab) of a foundling. The matn states it is established from one person. The commentary explains the tension between qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference), and clarifies the ruling for both the finder and other claimants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is establishing the foundling's lineage (nasab) through a claim. The ruling is that it is established by istihsan from a single claimant, regardless of whether they are the finder or someone else.
The text says 'يثبت نسبه من واحد' – lineage is established from one person.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
By strict qiyas, a claim from a non-finder should be rejected because it nullifies the finder's custodial right. Recognise that qiyas is the default method but can be overridden.
القياس أن لا يقبل دعوى غيره لأنه يتضمن إبطال حق الملتقط
3
Understand the istihsan override
Istihsan overrides qiyas due to the child's need for lineage – it is an acknowledgement benefiting the child, who is honored by lineage and disgraced without it. This is the core reasoning.
استحساناً لاحتياجه إليه – because of his need for it.
4
Distinguish claims by finder vs. non-finder
For the non-finder, the qiyas objection is about nullifying the finder's right. For the finder himself, the problem is contradiction of his own words (since taking the child as a foundling implies he is not the father). The correct view is that the finder's claim is on a different basis.
وجه القياس هنا غير وجه القياس في دعوى غير الملتقط
5
Recognize the implicit effect on custody
If a non-finder's claim is accepted, the claimant becomes more entitled to custody than the finder, because lineage implies priority in custody. This is an implicit consequence, not the primary purpose.
أفاد بثبوت النسب بدعوى غير الملتقط أن يكون أحق بحفظه من الملتقط

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويثبت نسبه من (واحد استحساناً لاحتياجه إليه. أطلقه فشمل الملتقط وغيره والقياس أن لا يقبل دعوى غيره لأنه يتضمن إبطال حق الملتقط. وجه الاستحسان أنه إقرار للصبي بما ينفعه لأنه يتشرف بالنسب ويعير بعدمه، ولو ادعاه الملتقط قيل يصح قياساً واستحساناً والأصح أنه على القياس والاستحسان لكن وجه القياس هنا غير وجه القياس في دعوى غير الملتقط، فوجهه في دعوى غير الملتقط تضمن إبطال حق الملتقط . ووجهه في دعوى الملتقط تناقض كلامه وتمامه في النهاية. وأفاد بثبوت النسب بدعوى غير الملتقط أن يكون أحق بحفظه من الملتقط ضرورة ثبوت النسب وكم من شيء يثبت ضمناً ولا قصداً الأصح. وأطلقه عن البينة فشمل ما إذا لم يبرهن استحساناً لما فيه من النظر
His statement: (And his lineage is established) from one [claimant] by istihsan because of his need for it. He made it absolute so it includes the finder and others. By qiyas, the claim of another should not be accepted because it entails nullifying the finder's right. The basis of istihsan is that it is an acknowledgement benefiting the child, because he is honored by lineage and disgraced without it. If the finder claims him, it is said it is valid by qiyas and istihsan, but the soundest view is that it is on qiyas and istihsan, but the basis of qiyas here is different from the basis of qiyas in the claim of a non-finder. For in the claim of a non-finder, it entails nullifying the finder's right. In the finder's claim, it is a contradiction of his own words. Its completion is in al-Nihayah. It indicates that by establishing lineage through the claim of a non-finder, he becomes more entitled to custody than the finder, necessarily because of the establishment of lineage. And many things are established implicitly rather than explicitly correctly. He made it absolute regarding evidence so it includes when there is no proof, by istihsan, due to the consideration involved.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on establishing the foundling's lineage (nasab). The matn states that lineage is established from one person. The commentator explains that this is by istihsan (juristic preference), due to the child's need for lineage. The ruling includes claims from the finder or anyone else. By qiyas, a claim from a non-finder should not be accepted because it would nullify the finder's custodial right. However, istihsan overrides qiyas, because acknowledging lineage benefits the child and avoiding the stigma of unknown parentage. If the finder himself claims lineage, there is a difference: some say it is valid by both qiyas and istihsan, but the correct view is that it involves contradiction (since the finder's prior act is inconsistent with claiming the child as his own). If a non-finder's claim is accepted, he becomes more entitled to custody than the finder, because lineage implies custody. This is an implicit rather than explicit effect. The ruling is absolute regarding evidence: even without proof, the claim is accepted by istihsan.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istihsān): Juristic preference; setting aside qiyas in favor of a more equitable ruling.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage; the child's legal filiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the claim of lineage from a non-finder accepted by istihsan despite qiyas?
A) To honor the finder
B) To benefit the child by giving him a lineage and preventing his being disgraced
C) To maintain consistency with qiyas
D) Because the finder always consents

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh uses istihsan to establish a foundling's lineage from any claimant, prioritizing the child's benefit over strict qiyas.

The Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Claimants for a Foundling's Lineage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on establishing lineage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for multiple claimants to establish nasab of a foundling and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the maximum number of claimants and women's claims.

Core Idea
Hanafi law uniquely allows multiple men and women to share parenthood of a foundling under specific conditions of common ownership or evidence.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a complex scenario: when two or more men claim a foundling as their son, and when women claim motherhood. The Hanafi school has internal disagreement on how many can be accepted, and whether women's claims are valid at all. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the ikhtilaf among the three leading imams, helping you navigate this exceptional area of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Condition
Both claimants must claim from a common slave-girl (jāriya mushtaraka). This is the condition from al-Khaniyya that limits the scenario to shared ownership.
If the child is free-born, this condition does not apply.
2
Note the Disagreement on Numbers
Abu Hanifa allows up to five claimants, Abu Yusuf only two, and Muhammad allows three. This ikhtilaf is based on different interpretations of the possible extent of shared paternity.
The passage does not provide justifications for these statements.
3
Examine Women's Claims
A woman's claim requires corroboration: her husband's confirmation, a midwife's testimony, or evidence. When two women both present evidence, Abu Hanifa accepts both as mothers, Abu Yusuf rejects both, and Muhammad has two narrations.
The testimony of a midwife is only sufficient if the woman has a husband who denies the birth; otherwise two male witnesses are needed.
4
Analyze the Role of Evidence and Dating
When two men present dated evidence, the child's age may decide the case. If age is ambiguous, the Sahibayn drop the date and divide the child; Abu Hanifa has two narrations: divide or give to the earlier date.
The preferred view (mufta bihi from al-Tatar Khaniyya) is division between claimants when evidence is equal.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
According to al-Tatar Khaniyya, the correct position is to divide the foundling between the claimants when evidence is equal. This applies to both men and women in the respective scenarios.
If one claimant precedes the other, the later must bring evidence stronger than the earlier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن اثنين أي ويثبت نسبه من اثنين إذا ادعياه معاً ولا مرجح لاستوائهما في السبب. وقيده في الخانية بأن يقول كل واحد منهما هو ولدي من جارية مشتركة بينهما. قيد بالاثنين لأن فيما زاد على الاثنين اختلافاً فروي عن الإمام أنه جوز إلى خمسة. وقال أبو يوسف : يثبت من اثنين ولا يثبت من أكثر من ذلك. وقال محمد أجوز الثلاثة ولا أجوز من ذلك ؛ كذا ذكره الأسبيجابي، ولم أر توجيه هذه الأقوال وقيد بدعوى الرجل لأن المدعي لو كان امرأة ادعت أنه ابنها فإن صدقها زوجها أو شهدت لها القابلة أو قامت البينة صحت دعوتها وإلا فلا ، لأن فيه حمل نسب الغير على الغير وأنه لا يجوز . ولو ادعت امرأتان وأقامت إحداهما البينة فهي أولى ،به وإن أقامتا جميعاً فهو ابنهما عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف لا يكون لواحدة منهما. وعن محمد روايتان في رواية أبي حفص يجعل ابنهما، وفي رواية أبي سليمان لا يجعل ابن واحدة منهما كذا في البدائع واعلم أن شهادة القابلة إنما يكتفى بها فيما إذا كان لها زوج منكر للولادة، أما إذا لم يكن لها زوج فلا بد من شهادة رجلين كما صرح به في الخانية وفيها لو أقامت إحداهما رجلين والأخرى امرأتين يجعل ابناً للذي شهد لها رجلان ولو ادعت امرأتان اللقيط أنه ابنهما كل واحدة منهما تقيم البينة على رجل على حدة بعينه أنها ولدته منه قال أبو حنيفة : يصير ولدهما من الرجلين جميعاً. وقالا : لا يصير ولدهما ولا ولد الرجلين .اهـ. وفي الظهيرية رجلان ادعيا نسب اللقيط وأقاما البينة وأرخت بينة كل واحدة منهما يقضي لمن يشهد له سن الصبي، فإن كان سن الصبي مشتبهاً لم يوافق كلا من التارخين فعلى قولهما يسقط اعتبار التاريخ ويقضى به بينهما باتفاق الروايات، وأما على قول أبي حنيفة فقد ذكر خواهر زاده أنه يقضي به بينهما في رواية أبي حفص، وفي رواية أبي سليمان يقضي لأقدمهما تاريخاً .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية أنه يقضي به بينهما في عامة الروايات وهو الصحيح. وقيدنا بكونهما ادعياه معاً لأنه لو سبقت دعوة أحدهما فهو ابنه لعدم النزاع، ولو ادعى الآخر بعده لا يقبل منه إلا ببينة لأن البينة أقوى؛
His statement: ('And from two' meaning and his lineage is established from two if they claim him together and there is no preponderance because they are equal in the cause. And he conditioned it in al-Khaniyya by each of them saying 'He is my son from a slave-girl common between them.' He restricted to two because regarding more than two there is disagreement: it is narrated from the Imam that he allowed up to five. Abu Yusuf said: It is established from two and not from more than that. Muhammad said: I allow three and do not allow more than that; thus mentioned by al-Asbijabi, but I have not seen justification for these statements. And he restricted it to a man's claim because if the claimant were a woman claiming that he is her son, then if her husband confirms her or a midwife testifies for her or she establishes evidence, her claim is valid; otherwise not, because that would involve attributing the lineage of another to another and that is not permissible. If two women claim and one of them establishes evidence, she is more entitled to him; if both establish evidence, then according to Abu Hanifa he is the son of both; according to Abu Yusuf he is not for either one of them; from Muhammad there are two narrations: in the narration of Abu Hafs he is made the son of both, in the narration of Abu Sulayman he is not made the son of either one of them, as in al-Bada'i. Know that the testimony of the midwife is only accepted if the woman has a husband who denies the birth; if she has no husband, then the testimony of two men is necessary, as clarified in al-Khaniyya. And in it: if one woman brings two men and the other brings two women, the foundling is made the son of the one for whom two men testified. If two women claim the foundling, each establishing evidence from a specific man that she gave birth to him from that man, Abu Hanifa said: He becomes the son of both from both men; the two said: He does not become the son of either nor the son of the men. In al-Zahiriyya: Two men claim the lineage of the foundling and both establish evidence, and the evidence of each is dated: judgment is given to the one whose evidence matches the age of the boy. If the boy's age is ambiguous and does not match either date, then according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the consideration of date drops and he is judged between both by unanimous narrations; according to Abu Hanifa, Khwar zada mentioned that he is judged to be between both in the narration of Abu Hafs, and in the narration of Abu Sulayman he is judged to be for the one with the earlier date. In al-Tatar Khaniyya it is stated that he is judged to be between both in all narrations, which is correct. And we conditioned that they claim him together, because if the claim of one preceded, he is his son due to lack of dispute; if the other claims later, it is not accepted from him without evidence because evidence is stronger.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the establishment of lineage (nasab) of a foundling when two men claim him simultaneously. The author explains that the matn restricts to two claimants, but there is ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams on allowing more than two: Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed up to five, Abu Yusuf only two, and Muhammad allowed up to three. The condition that both must claim from a common slave-girl is from al-Khaniyya. The discussion then extends to women claimants: a woman's claim requires corroboration by her husband, a midwife, or evidence; two women with evidence can both be mothers according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Abu Yusuf, with two narrations from Muhammad. The testimony of a midwife is only sufficient when there is a husband denying birth; otherwise two men are needed. When both women bring evidence from specific men, Abu Hanifa allows nasab from both men, but the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not. When two men bring evidence with dates, the age of the child can be a tiebreaker; if ambiguous, the Sahibayn drop the date and divide the child between them, while Abu Hanifa has two narrations: divide or give to the earlier date. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is division between them. Finally, if one claimant precedes the other, the later claimant needs stronger evidence.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling, an abandoned child of unknown lineage.
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity/maternity established by claim and evidence.
جارية مشتركة (jāriya mushtaraka): A female slave owned jointly by the two claimants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi (preferred view) when two men present equal evidence and the child's age is ambiguous?
A) The child is given to the older claimant.
B) The child is divided between the claimants.
C) The child is given to the one whose evidence matches his age.
D) The claim is invalid and the child remains without lineage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely permits multiple parenthood for a foundling under specific conditions, but there is internal disagreement on the maximum number and on women's claims, with the preferred view being division between claimants when evidence is equal.

Ruling on Picking Up a Foundling
Statement of ruling and conditions for picking up a foundling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on picking up a foundling and identify the conditions that make it obligatory or valid.

Core Idea
Picking up a foundling is obligatory when harm is feared; otherwise it is valid but not required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq clarifies the ruling of iltiqat al-laqit. The author states that the act is valid (sahih) and becomes obligatory (wajib) if the child is in danger. In addition, it outlines the qualifications of the picker. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is picking up a foundling (iltiqat al-laqit). The ruling: it is valid (sahih) and becomes obligatory (wajib) if one fears the child may be lost.
Obligation arises from the need to preserve life.
2
Determine the picker's conditions
The picker (multaqit) must be mukallaf (adult and sane). A child or insane person cannot validly pick up a foundling.
Mukallaf is one who is legally responsible for his actions.
3
Recognize what is NOT required
It is not required that the picker be Muslim, upright (adl), or financially sensible (rashid). The text indicates that even a disbeliever, a corrupt person, or a restricted slave can validly pick up.
The text points to later discussions for full justification.
4
Understand the implications
This ruling shows the Hanafi school’s emphasis on the child’s welfare. Any capable person (mukallaf) may rescue the child, regardless of their own status.
This prevents harm to the child from delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح التقاط ووجب إن خاف الضياع أي لزم وفيه إيماء إلى أنه يشترط في الملتقط كونه مكلفاً فلا الصبي والمجنون، ولا يشترط أن يكون مسلماً عدلاً رشيداً لما سيأتي من أن التقاط الكافر صحيح والفاسق أولى وأن العبد المحجور عليه يصح التقاطه أيضاً فالمحجور عليه بالسفه أولى.
Picking up is valid and becomes obligatory if one fears loss, i.e. it is binding. And in it there is an indication that it is a condition in the picker that he be legally responsible (mukallaf), hence not a child or a madman. And it is not a condition that he be Muslim, upright, or sensible, because it will come that the picking up of a disbeliever is valid, and a corrupt person is better, and that a restricted slave's picking up is also valid, so the one restricted due to foolishness is even more appropriate.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage states the ruling (hukm) of picking up a foundling (iltiqat al-laqit). The base ruling is that it is valid (sahih) and becomes obligatory (wajib) if one fears the child may be lost. The text indicates that the picker must be mukallaf (adult and sane), so a child or insane person cannot validly pick up. However, it is not necessary that the picker be Muslim, just ('adl), or free from financial restriction (rashid); a non-Muslim, a sinful person, a slave under guardianship, or a person under interdiction for foolishness (safah) can validly pick up. The passage points to later discussions to support these points.

Key Terms
الالتقاط (al-iltiqāṭ): The act of taking up and caring for a foundling.
الملتقط (al-multaqiṭ): The person who picks up the foundling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does picking up a foundling become obligatory (wajib)?
A) Always
B) When one fears the child may be lost
C) Only if the child is Muslim
D) Only if the picker is Muslim

Key Takeaway
Picking up a foundling is valid from any mukallaf, and becomes obligatory when the child's safety is at risk.

Priority by Mark and Dhimmi Claimant in Foundling Cases
Rules on preponderance by mark, Dhimmi claim, and khuntha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for priority by mark, identify the weakest preponderating factor, and apply the Hanafi rules for establishing nasab when a Dhimmi claims a foundling.

Core Idea
Mark is the weakest preponderating factor; a Dhimmi can claim a foundling only if no Muslim claims, and the child remains Muslim.

In disputes over a foundling, claimants may offer different types of evidence. One type is describing a physical mark. However, the mark is not strong evidence; it is only considered when no stronger factor is present. Additionally, a Dhimmi (non-Muslim protected subject) can establish lineage of a foundling, but with important conditions regarding the child's religion. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies these intricate rules and the hierarchy of preponderating factors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mark condition
Note that priority by mark requires the description to match the child exactly; if it does not match, the child is shared. Also, this rule applies only to foundlings, not to lost property or other disputes.
2
List stronger factors
The mark is the weakest factor. Identify factors that outweigh it: (1) proof (bayyina), (2) being Muslim, (3) being in possession, (4) being free. Understand the hierarchy.
This hierarchy is specific to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the khuntha rule
When one claimant claims the foundling is a son and another claims a daughter, and the child is a hermaphrodite (khuntha): if ambiguous, divide between them; if not ambiguous, give to the one whose claim matches the apparent sex.
The text cites al-Tatar Khaniyya and al-Quduri.
4
Explain the Dhimmi claim
A Dhimmi can establish nasab of a foundling only if no Muslim claims. The child is Muslim unless found in a locality of the People of the Book (i.e., Dhimmi territory). This is based on istihsan.
This is a Hanafi-specific ruling.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on common mother
When two men claim a child from a common slave-girl, the passage discusses whether the mother must be the same. Abu Hanifa does not require unity of mother; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do. Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): under Abu Hanifa, nasab can be established from multiple men even with different mothers; under the Sahibayn, it cannot.
The passage also reports a variant from al-Khaniyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن وصف أحدهما علامة به أي بالولد قوله : (فهو أحق به يعني إذا وافقها لأن الظاهر شاهد له لموافقة العلامة كلامه. قيد باللقيط لأن صاحب العلامة في اللقطة لا يترجح عند التنازع لأن الترجيح عند وجود سبب الاستحقاق وقد وجد في اللقيط وهو الدعوة دون اللقطة، وكذا لو تنازع خارجان عيناً في يد ثالث وذكر أحدهما علامة فإنه لا ترجيح له، وقيدنا بالموافقة لأنه لو وصف أحدهما العلامة ولم يصب فلا ترجيح وهو ابنهما، وكذا لو وصف أحدهما وأصاب في البعض وأخطأ في البعض فهو ابنهما، وإن وصفا ولم يصب واحد منهما فهو ابنهما، ولو وصفا وأصاب أحدهما دون الآخر قضي للذي أصاب؛ كذا في الظهيرية. ثم اعلم أن العلامة مرجحة عند عدم مرجح أقوى منها فيقدم ذو البرهان على ذي العلامة والمسلم على الذمي ذي العلامة، وظاهر ما في فتح القدير تقديم ذي اليد على الخارج ذي العلامة، وينبغي تقديم الحر على العبد ذي العلامة فعلم أنها أضعف المرجحات وفي التتارخانية : وإذا ادعى اللقيط رجلان ادعى أحدهما أنه ابنه والآخر أنه ابنته فإذا هو خنثى فإن كان مشكلاً قضى به بينهما، وإن لم يكن مشكلاً حكم به لمن ادعى أنه ابنه .اهـ. وفيها عن القدوري : لو شهد للمسلم ذميان وللذمي مسلمان قضى به للمسلم. قوله : (ومن ذمي وهو مسلم إن لم يكن في مكان أهل الذمة أي يثبت النسب من ذمي عند عدم دعوى مسلم ويكون اللقيط مسلماً إن لم يكن في مكان أهل الذمة، وهذا استحسان الذي فيها : لو ادعى رجلان معاً كل واحد منهما يقول هو ولدي من جارية مشتركة بينهما ثبت نسبه وصار ولداً لهما، وهذا كما ترى لا يفيد تقييداً أصلاً. ثم رأيت في التتارخانية : لو عين كل واحد منهما امرأة أخرى قضي بالولد بينهما. وهل يثبت نسب الولد من المرأتين؟ على قياس قول أبي حنيفة يثبت وعلى قولهما لا يثبت وقال قبله لو ادعته امرأتان كل واحدة منهما تقيم البينة على رجل على حدة معينة أنها ولدته منه قال أبو حنيفة : يصير ولدهما من الرجلين جميعاً. وقالا : يصير ولدهما لا ولد الرجلين اهـ. وهذا كما ترى صريح في أن اتحاد الوالدة ليس شرطاً في ثبوته من متعدد، نعم المذكور في الخانية عنهما أنه لا يصير ولدهما ولا ولد الرجلين. قوله : ( وإن لم يكن مشكلاً حكم به لمن ادعى أنه ابنه قال المقدسي: ينبغي إن وافق وإلا فلمن
His statement: ('And if one of them describes a mark') meaning of the child. His statement: ('then he is more entitled to him') meaning if it matches, because the apparent evidence is a witness for him due to the match of the mark with his statement. It is restricted to the foundling because the owner of a mark in lost property (luqata) does not have preference in a dispute, because preference is given when there is a cause of entitlement, and that exists in the foundling (the claim) but not in lost property. Similarly, if two outsiders dispute over an article in the hand of a third and one mentions a mark, there is no preference for him. And we condition matching, because if one describes the mark and does not get it right, there is no preference and the child is shared between them. Likewise, if one describes and gets it partly right and partly wrong, the child is shared. If both describe and neither gets it right, the child is shared. If both describe and one gets it right and the other not, judgment is given to the one who got it right; as in al-Zahiriyya. Then know that a mark is a preponderating factor only when there is no stronger factor: the one with proof (bayyina) is preferred over the one with a mark, a Muslim over a Dhimmi with a mark, and the apparent ruling in Fath al-Qadir gives preference to the one in possession over an outsider with a mark. It is appropriate to prefer a free person over a slave with a mark. It becomes clear that the mark is the weakest of preponderating factors. In al-Tatar Khaniyya: If two men claim a foundling, one claiming he is his son and the other that he is his daughter, and it turns out to be a hermaphrodite (khuntha): if it is ambiguous, it is judged between them; if not ambiguous, judgment is given to the one who claimed it is his son. In it (also) from al-Quduri: If a Muslim brings two Dhimmis as witnesses and a Dhimmi brings two Muslims, judgment is given to the Muslim. His statement: ('And from a Dhimmi and he is a Muslim unless he is in a locality of the People of the Book') meaning that lineage is established from a Dhimmi in the absence of a Muslim's claim, and the foundling is a Muslim unless he is found in a locality of the People of the Book; this is istihsan. What is in it: If two men claim together, each saying 'he is my son from a slave-girl common between us', his lineage is established and he becomes a son to both. That, as you see, does not provide any restriction. Then I saw in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If each specifies a different woman, the child is judged between them. Does the lineage of the child become established from the two women? By analogy with Abu Hanifa's statement, it is established; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not. He said before: If two women claim the foundling, each establishing evidence from a specific man that she gave birth to him from that man, Abu Hanifa said: He becomes their son from both men; the two said: He becomes their son but not the son of the two men. This is explicit that unity of the mother is not a condition for its establishment from multiple parties. Yes, what is mentioned in al-Khaniyya from the two is that he does not become their son nor the son of the two men. His statement: ('And if it is not ambiguous, judgment is given to the one who claimed it is his son'). Al-Maqdisi said: It should be conditional on agreement, otherwise...
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage combines several rulings: (1) Priority by mark (alama): If one claimant describes a distinguishing mark of the foundling and matches it, he is given preference, but only in the case of a foundling (not lost property). The mark is the weakest of all preponderating factors; stronger factors include proof (bayyina), being Muslim, being in possession, being free. (2) Gender ambiguity (khuntha): If a child is a hermaphrodite and one claims he is a son and another a daughter, if the child's sex is ambiguous it is divided between them; if clear, it goes to the one whose description matches. (3) Dhimmi claimant: A Dhimmi can establish nasab only if no Muslim claims. The child is Muslim unless found in a Dhimmi settlement. (4) Return to earlier condition about common mother: The passage then repeats the condition of common slave-girl, clarifying that specifying different women does not prevent nasab from multiple men according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn differ. It stresses that unity of mother is not a condition in Abu Hanifa's view, though al-Khaniyya narrates otherwise.

Key Terms
العلامة (al-ʿalāma): A distinguishing physical mark or description of the child that one claimant mentions.
الخنثى (al-khunthā): A hermaphrodite child with ambiguous gender.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who lives under protection (dhimma).
البرهان (al-burhān): Proof or evidence, specifically witnesses (bayyina) in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the weakest preponderating factor in a foundling dispute?
A) Being Muslim
B) Having a matching mark
C) Bringing two witnesses
D) Being free

Key Takeaway
Mark is the weakest preponderating factor; a Dhimmi can claim a foundling only when no Muslim claims, and the child's religion follows the territory.

The Foundling's Freedom and Religion: Place vs Finder
Ruling on foundling's freedom and religion with proof and intra-Hanafi variations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a foundling's default status as free and Muslim, and explain the preferred opinion (zāhir al-riwāya) that his religion follows the place of discovery rather than the finder.

Core Idea
A foundling is free; his religion follows the place, not the finder.

When a child of unknown parentage is found, the law must assign a default status. The Hanafi school presumes the child is free and Muslim, based on the principle that the abode (dār) confers Islamic identity. But what if the place is a non-Muslim village or the finder is a dhimmi? This lesson unpacks the rulings and the internal Hanafi debate on whether the finder's religion or the location determines the child's religious status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The passage begins by asserting the foundational rule: the foundling (al-laqīṭ) is free (ḥurr) and does not become a slave except with clear evidence. Note that any property found with him belongs to him.
This default protects the child's freedom and property.
2
Understand the role of the finder
The finder (al-multaqit) is a custodian, not an owner. He cannot marry the foundling on his/her behalf because that would involve rights and potential harm. The passage highlights that the finder's claim of lineage benefits the child, but nullifying the child's Islam by the abode harms him.
Care for the child's best interest is paramount.
3
Analyze the religious status based on location
The core issue: in which scenario is the foundling considered Muslim or dhimmi? The passage presents four scenarios: (1) Muslim finder in Muslim place → Muslim; (2) dhimmi finder in dhimmi place → dhimmi; (3) Muslim finder in dhimmi place; (4) dhimmi finder in Muslim place. For (3) and (4), there are multiple narrations.
The zāhir al-riwāya (apparent narration) gives weight to the place.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The commentator lists four views: (a) place considered in all cases (zāhir al-riwāya); (b) finder considered (Ibn Samā‘a); (c) Islam considered out of regard for the minor; (d) appearance/dress considered (Kifāyat al-Bayhaqī). The preferred view (mukhtār) is that of place.
The dalīl for considering appearance is Quran 2:273 and 55:41, cited in Kifāyat al-Bayhaqī.
5
Apply the ruling when the foundling matures
If the foundling reaches maturity as a disbeliever, and was found in a Muslim city, he is compelled to Islam by istiḥsān. Additionally, if a dhimmi claims the foundling is his son, evidence must come from Muslim witnesses; testimony from dhimmis is not accepted because the child is already ruled Muslim.
This shows the strength of the default Islamic status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب اللقيط وهو حر ولا يرق إلا ببينة وإن وجد معه مال فهو له ولا يصح للملتقط عليه نكاح أحدهما يعتبر لأن دعواه تتضمن النسب وهو نافع للصغير وإبطال الإسلام الثابت بالدار وهو يضره فصحت دعوته فيما ينفعه دون ما يضره والمراد من مكان أهل الذمة قرية من قراهم أو بيعة أو كنيسة. قال في الهداية وهذا الجواب فيما إذا كان الواجد ذمياً رواية واحدة، وإن كان الواجد مسلماً في هذا المكان أو ذمياً في مكان المسلمين اختلفت الرواية فيه ففي كتاب اللقيط اعتبر المكان لسبقه، وفي كتاب الدعوى في بعض النسخ اعتبر الواجد وهو رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد لقوة اليد ألا ترى أن تبعية الأبوين فوق تبعية الدار حتى إذا سبى مع الصغير كافراً. وفي بعض نسخه اعتبر الإسلام نظراً للصغير. وفي النهاية حاصلها على أربعة أوجه : أحدها أن يجده مسلم في مكان المسلمين فهو مسلم. ثانيها أن يجده كافر في مكانهم فهو كافر. ثالثها أن يجده كافر في مكان المسلمين. رابعها عكسه ففيه روايتان ففي كتاب اللقيط العبرة للمكان فيهما، وفي رواية ابن سماعة العبرة للواجد فيهما. وفي فتح القدير : ولا ينبغي أن يعدل عما في بعض النسخ من اعتبار الإسلام أي ما يصير الولد به مسلماً نظراً للصغير .اهـ. وظاهر كلام المصنف أنه إنما يعتبر مكان أهل الذمة إذا كان الواجد ذمياً ومفهومه إن يكون مسلماً في الصور الثلاث ذمياً في صورة واحدة ولا يعدل عنه كما ذكرنا. وفي كفاية البيهقي قيل يعتبر بالسيما والزي لأنه حجة قال الله تعالى نعرفهم بسيماهم [البقرة : ٢٧٣] وقال يعرف المجرمون بسيماهم ﴾ [الرحمن: ٤١] وفي المبسوط : كما لو اختلط الكفار يعني موتانا بموتاهم فإنه يعتبر بالزي والعلامة، ولو فتحت القسطنطونية فوجد فيها شيخ يعلم صبياناً حوله القرآن يزعم أنه مسلم يجب أن يؤخذ بقوله؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وذكر في الخانية الروايات الأربع وصرح في المختار بأن ظاهر الرواية اعتبار المكان. وفي الخانية : ولو أدرك اللقيط كافراً فإن كان الملتقط وجده في مصر من أمصار المسلمين فإنه يحبس ويجير على الإسلام استحساناً. واختلفوا في موضع القياس والاستحسان قال بعضهم : القياس والاستحسان في قتله إذا لم يسلم في القياس يقتل، وفي الاستحسان لا يقتل. وقال بعضهم : الاستحسان والقياس في الجبر على الإسلام في القياس لا يجبر على الإسلام وترك على الكفر بالحرية وفي الاستحسان يجبر على الإسلام ولا يترك على الكفر وهو الصحيح .اهـ. ثم اعلم أن ابن الذمي اللقيط إنما يكون مسلماً إذا لم يقم بينة أنه ابنه فإن برهن بشهود مسلمين قضي له به وصار تبعاً له في دينه، وإن أقام بينة من أهل الذمة لا يكون ذمياً لأنا حكمنا بإسلامه فلا يبطل هذا الحكم بهذه البينة لأنها شهادة قامت في حكم الدين على مسلم فلا تقبل؛ كذا في الخانية.
Book of the Foundling. He is free and does not become a slave except with evidence. And if property is found with him, it belongs to him. It is not valid for the finder to contract marriage on his behalf. One of them is considered because his claim includes lineage, which benefits the young child, while nullifying the Islam established by the abode harms him. So his claim is valid in what benefits him, not in what harms him. What is meant by 'place of the People of the Book' is a village of their villages, or a church or synagogue. He said in al-Hidayah: This answer is when the finder is a dhimmi, in one narration. And if the finder is a Muslim in this place, or a dhimmi in a place of Muslims, the narration differs. In the Book of the Foundling, the place is considered due to its precedence. In the Book of Claims, in some copies, the finder is considered, which is the narration of Ibn Samā‘a from Muhammad, because of the strength of possession. Do you not see that the following of the parents is above the following of the abode, such that when [a child] is captured with a minor [who is] a disbeliever? And in some copies, Islam is considered out of regard for the minor. In al-Nihāyah: The summary is four aspects: First, that a Muslim finds him in a place of Muslims; he is Muslim. Second, that a disbeliever finds him in their place; he is a disbeliever. Third, that a disbeliever finds him in a place of Muslims. Fourth, the opposite of that. There are two narrations: in the Book of the Foundling, the consideration is for the place in both; in the narration of Ibn Samā‘a, the consideration is for the finder in both. In Fatḥ al-Qadīr: It is not appropriate to depart from that which is in some copies of considering Islam, i.e., that by which the child becomes Muslim, out of regard for the minor. The apparent meaning of the author's words is that the place of the People of the Book is considered only when the finder is a dhimmi. Its implication is that [the foundling] is Muslim in three scenarios and dhimmi in one scenario, and one should not depart from this as we mentioned. In Kifāyat al-Bayhaqī, it is said that consideration is given to appearance and dress because it is proof. Allah said, 'We will know them by their marks' (Quran 2:273) and 'The criminals will be known by their marks' (Quran 55:41). In al-Mabsūṭ: As if the disbelievers, meaning our dead and their dead, are mixed, then consideration is given to dress and sign. And if Constantinople were conquered and a teacher was found teaching children the Quran around him, claiming to be Muslim, his statement must be accepted. Similarly in Fatḥ al-Qadīr. In al-Khāniyya, the four narrations are mentioned, and in al-Mukhtār, it is explicitly stated that the apparent of the narration is consideration of place. In al-Khāniyya: If the foundling reaches [maturity] as a disbeliever, and the finder found him in a city of the cities of Muslims, then he is imprisoned and compelled to Islam by istiḥsān. They differed regarding the place of qiyās and istiḥsān. Some said: The qiyās and istiḥsān regarding his killing if he does not accept Islam: in qiyās he is killed, in istiḥsān he is not killed. Others said: The istiḥsān and qiyās regarding compulsion to Islam: in qiyās he is not compelled to Islam and left on disbelief due to freedom, and in istiḥsān he is compelled to Islam and not left on disbelief, and this is correct. Then know that the foundling son of a dhimmi is Muslim only if he does not provide evidence that he is his son. For if he proves with Muslim witnesses, judgment is given to him and he follows him in his religion. And if he provides evidence from the People of the Book, he does not become a dhimmi because we have ruled his Islam; this ruling is not invalidated by this evidence because it is testimony established in a matter of religion against a Muslim, so it is not accepted. Similarly in al-Khāniyya.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqā’iq, explaining the chapter on the foundling (al-laqīṭ). The passage type is a ruling with proof, where the commentator expands on the foundational ruling that a foundling is free and does not become a slave except with clear evidence. The commentator first states the default: a foundling is free, and any property found with him belongs to him. The finder cannot contract a marriage on behalf of the foundling because that would involve rights and obligations that may conflict with the child's interests. The text then delves into the more nuanced question of the foundling's religious status. The key principle is that a foundling is presumed Muslim and free because the abode (dār) in which he is found—if it is a Muslim land—confers Islamic status. However, certain exceptions arise depending on the finder's religion and the specific location. The commentator presents the ruling from al-Hidāyah, noting that when the finder is a dhimmi in a dhimmi location, it is a single narration that the child is dhimmi. In other scenarios (Muslim finder in a dhimmi place, dhimmi finder in a Muslim place), there are multiple narrations. The preferred opinion (zāhir al-riwāya) is that consideration is given to the place, not the finder. This is supported by the principle that the child follows the more honorable status (Islam). The passage also discusses the case when the foundling grows up and remains a disbeliever; by istiḥsān, he is compelled to Islam if found in a Muslim city. Additionally, if a dhimmi claims the foundling is his son, evidence from Muslim witnesses is accepted; evidence from dhimmis is not, because the child has already been declared Muslim by virtue of the abode. The commentator preserves the intra-Hanafi differences (e.g., Ibn Samā‘a's narration) but ultimately upholds the mukhtār position of considering the place.

Key Terms
اللقيط (al-laqīṭ): A foundling: a child of unknown parentage found abandoned in a place, who is presumed free and Muslim.
الملتقط (al-multaqit): The finder of the foundling, who has physical custody but not ownership.
أهل الذمة (ahl al-dhimma): Non-Muslim subjects of the Islamic state who have a covenant (dhimma) guaranteeing protection and religious freedom, typically Christians, Jews, and Zoroastrians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Muslim finds a foundling in a village of dhimmis, what is the Hanafi ruling (zāhir al-riwāya) regarding the foundling's religion?
A) The foundling is Muslim because the finder is Muslim.
B) The foundling is dhimmi because the place is dhimmi.
C) The foundling is Muslim because Islam takes precedence.
D) The foundling's religion is unknown until he reaches puberty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the default status of a foundling is free and Muslim by virtue of the abode; the place of discovery is the primary determinant of religion, overriding the finder's religion in the relied-upon view.

Slave's Claim of Paternity over a Foundling: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on status of foundling claimed by a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the status of a foundling claimed by a slave, and explain the preponderant view.

Core Idea
A slave's claim of paternity does not invalidate the foundling's freedom established by the abode.

This lesson explores a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf on the status of a foundling when a slave claims him as his son from a slave wife. The core issue is whether the child's freedom, presumed from being found in a Muslim abode, can be overturned by a slave's claim and mutual confirmation with the master. The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, explains the reasoning and ultimately prefers Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, state Muhammad's view that the foundling remains free. Then, state Abu Yusuf's view that the foundling becomes a slave to the mother's master.
Both are within the Hanafi school; the difference turns on the weight given to established freedom vs. logical necessity.
2
Examine Muhammad's evidence
Muhammad argues that freedom established by the abode is a certainty and cannot be negated by doubt (shakk). Moreover, it is possible for the child to have been manumitted before or after birth, so the necessity of slavery is not absolute.
The principle 'certainty is not removed by doubt' is key here.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's evidence
Abu Yusuf argues that since both parents are slaves, the child necessarily follows the mother's status. Birth from two slaves cannot produce a free child without manumission, so the child must be a slave.
This is based on the general rule that a child's status follows the mother's.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response (supporting Muhammad) is that the necessity is not absolute; manumission is possible, so the existing freedom prevails over doubt. The doubt does not overturn the established freedom.
The commentator notes that al-Tabyīn indicates preference for Muhammad's view.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Muhammad, the foundling remains free and cannot be enslaved. Under Abu Yusuf, the foundling becomes a slave to the master of the mother. The mufta bihi is Muhammad's view.
The difference affects the child's status and the rights of the parties involved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن عبد وهو حر) أي يثبت نسبه من عبد ادعى أنه ابنه لأنه ينفعه وكان حراً لأن المملوك قد تلد له الحرة فلا تبطل الحرية الظاهرة بالشك، وقدمنا أن الحر في دعوته اللقيط أولى من العبد كما أن المسلم أولى من الذمي ترجيحاً لما هو إلا نظر في حقه. أطلق في قوله وهو حر» فشمل ما إذا قال العبد هو ابني من زوجتي وهي أمة فصدقه مولاها لأنه حر باعتبار الأصل فلا تبطل الحرية بتصادق العبد وسيدها، وهذا قول محمد وقال أبو يوسف : يكون عبداً لسيدها لأن الأمة أمه فإذا ثبت النسب منها ثبت ما هو من ضروراته وهو الرق إذ يستحيل أن يكون المولود بين رقيقين حراً بخلاف الذمي على ما بينا قلنا لا يستحيل ذلك لأنه يجوز عتقه قبل الانفصال وبعده فلا تبطل الحرية الثابتة بالدار بالشك؛ كذا في التبيين وظاهره ترجیح قول محمد. وفي آخر جامع الفصولين قيل ؛ قد يكون الولد حراً من زوجين قنين بلا تحرير ووصية وصورته أن يكون للحر ولد وهو قن لأجنبي فزوج الأب أمته من ولده برضا مولاه فولدت الأمة ولداً لأنه ولد ولد المولى اهـ. وفي التبيين ولو ادعاه حران أحدهما أنه ابنه من هذه الحرة الحرة أولى لكونه أكثر إثباتاً لكونه يثبت جميع أحكام الأمة فالذي يدعي من النسب ولو كانت الأمة سرية له لأنه يثبت الأحكام من جانب والآخر من جانبين فكان أولى.
His saying: 'And from a slave, and he is free' means that his lineage is established from a slave who claims that he is his son, because it benefits him and he was free. For it is possible for a female free person to give birth to a slave's child, so the apparent freedom is not invalidated by doubt. We have already stated that a free person in claiming the foundling is preferable to a slave, just as a Muslim is preferable to a dhimmi, giving preference to what is in his interest. His saying 'and he is free' is general, covering the case where the slave says 'he is my son from my wife who is a slave-girl' and her master confirms him, because he is free by origin, so the freedom is not invalidated by the mutual confirmation of the slave and his master. This is the statement of Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: He is a slave to her master because the slave-girl is his mother; when lineage is established from her, what is necessitated by it is established, which is slavery, because it is impossible for a child born between two slaves to be free, contrary to the dhimmi as we explained. We say: This is not impossible because it is permissible for him to be freed before separation and after, so the freedom established by the abode is not invalidated by doubt. This is in al-Tabyīn, and its apparent meaning is a preference for the statement of Muhammad. In the end of Jāmi‘ al-Fusūlayn, it is said: A child may be free from two slave parents without manumission or bequest. Its form is that a free man has a son who is a slave to a third party; then the father marries his slave-girl to his son with the master's consent, and the slave-girl gives birth to a child, because it is the child of the master's child. In al-Tabyīn: If two free men claim him, one claiming he is his son from this free woman, the free one is more entitled because it establishes more, as it establishes all the rulings of the [free] mother. So the one who claims lineage, even if the slave-girl is his concubine, because he establishes rulings from one side, while the other establishes from two sides, so the former is more entitled.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the claim of a slave that a foundling is his son. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute with ruling. The commentator explains the matn's phrase 'and from a slave, and he is free,' meaning that if a slave claims the foundling is his son, the foundling remains free. The reasoning is that it is possible for a free woman to bear a slave's child, so the apparent freedom (by virtue of the abode) is not overridden by mere doubt. The slave's claim benefits the foundling (establishing lineage), so it is accepted regarding lineage but does not affect his freedom. The commentator then presents the ikhtilāf between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf: Muhammad holds that even if the slave says the mother is also a slave and the master confirms, the child remains free, because freedom is established by the abode and cannot be negated by the testimony of slaves. Abu Yusuf argues that since both parents are slaves, the child must be a slave, as it is impossible for a child of two slaves to be free. The Hanafi response (supporting Muhammad) is that it is not impossible because the child could have been manumitted before or after birth; thus doubt does not override the established freedom. The commentary also discusses a scenario from Jāmi‘ al-Fusūlayn where a child can be free from two slave parents, supporting Muhammad's view. Finally, the passage addresses a case where two free men claim the foundling: one claims he is his son from a free woman, the other from a slave concubine. The one claiming from a free woman is preferred because it establishes more (both lineage and full freedom).

Key Terms
الحرية الظاهرة (al-ḥurriyya al-ẓāhira): Apparent freedom: the legal status of freedom that is presumed for the foundling by virtue of being found in a Muslim abode.
تصادق (taṣāduq): Mutual confirmation: when the slave and his master both affirm the slave's claim that the foundling is his son from a slave wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding a foundling claimed by a slave whose wife is a slave, what is Muhammad's ruling?
A) The foundling becomes a slave because both parents are slaves.
B) The foundling remains free because freedom established by the abode is not invalidated by doubt.
C) The foundling is free only if the master manumits him.
D) The foundling's status is determined by the finder's claim.

Key Takeaway
The foundling's freedom, established by the abode, is not overturned by a slave's claim of paternity, even when both parents are slaves, because certainty cannot be invalidated by doubt.

Evidentiary Rules for Enslaving a Foundling
Evidentiary rule for claiming a foundling as a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi evidentiary rule for claiming a foundling as a slave, and distinguish between cases where the finder admits or does not admit the child is a foundling.

Core Idea
A foundling is presumptively free; only clear evidence overcomes that status.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq expands on the principle that a foundling does not become a slave without evidence. The commentary addresses the finder's claim, the role of possession, and the distinction between lineage and slave claims, as well as the conditions for accepting evidence and the foundling's own admission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default status of the foundling
The foundling is presumed free (ḥurr aṣlī). The matn states 'he does not become a slave except with evidence,' establishing a clear default.
2
Distinguish two scenarios for the finder's claim
If the finder does not admit the child is a foundling, his word is taken because of possession. If he admits it is a foundling, his claim requires evidence.
This distinction is from al-Muḥīṭ.
3
Understand why lineage claims differ from slave claims
The finder's hand (possession) is for the child's benefit. A lineage claim benefits the child, so it overrides the finder's hand without evidence. A slavery claim harms the child, so evidence is required.
The illa (effective cause) is the benefit vs. harm.
4
Apply conditions for evidence and the foundling's own admission
Evidence must come from Muslim witnesses (unless the foundling is from a dhimmi area). The foundling's admission of slavery before puberty is invalid; after puberty, it is valid only if no prior judgment of freedom exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يرق إلا ببينة لأنه حر ظاهراً فإذا أقام بينة أنه عبده قبلت وكان عبده، يقال هذه البينة ليست على خصم فلا تقبل لأن الملتقط خصم لأنه أحق بثبوت يده عليه فلا تزول إلا ببينة هنا، وإنما قلنا هنا كيلا ينقص بما إذا ادعى خارج نسبه فإن يده تزول بلا بينة على الأوجه، والفرق أن يده اعتبرت لمنفعة الولد وفي دعوى النسب منفعة تفوق المنفعة التي أو جبت اعتبار يد الملتقط فتزال لحصول ما يفوق المقصود من اعتبارها، وهنا ليس دعوى العبدية كذلك بل هو بما يضره لتبديل صفة المالكية بالمملوكية فلا تزال إلا ببينة، ويشترط في قبولها إسلامهم لأنه مسلم بالدار وباليد فلا يحكم عليه بشهادة الكفار إلا إذا اعتبر كافراً بوجوده في موضع أهل الذمة على ما بينا . وفي المحيط : وإن ادعى الملتقط أنه عبده إن لم يقر بأنه لقيط فالقول قوله لأن الصغير في يده، وإن أقر أنه لقيط لا يصدق في دعواه إلا ببينة . قيد بالبينة لأنه لا يرق بإقراره المدعيه فلو صدقه اللقيط قبل البلوغ لا . تصديقه لأنه يضر يسمع به نفسه بعد الحكم بالحرية بخلاف ما إذا كان صغيراً في يد رجل فادعى أنه عبده وصدقه الغلام فإنه يكون عبداً له وإن لم يدرك لأنه لم يعرف إلا في يده وإن رد لا يصح لقيام يده من وجه، وإن بلغ فأقر أنه عبد فلان وفلان يدعيه إن كان قبل أن يقضي عليه بما لا يقضى به إلا على الأحرار كالحد الكامل ونحوه صح إقراره وصار عبداً لأنه غير متهم فيه، وإن كان القضاء ذلك لا يقبل ولا يصير به عبداً لأن فيه إبطال حكم الحاكم، ولأنه مكذب بنحو في ذلك شرعاً فهو كما لو كذبه الذي أقر له بالرق. ولو كان اللقيط امرأة فأقرت بالرق بعدما كبرت أو كان بعد التزوج صح وكانت أمة للمقر ،له ولا تصدق في إبطال النكاح لأن بعد
His saying: 'And he does not become a slave except with evidence' because he is apparently free. So if he brings evidence that he is his slave, it is accepted and he becomes his slave. It is said: This evidence is not against an opponent, so it is not accepted, because the finder is an opponent, as he is more entitled to the establishment of his hand over him, so it is not removed except with evidence here. We only said here so as not to fall short in the case where an outsider claims lineage, for his hand is removed without evidence in the prevailing opinions. The difference is that his hand is considered for the benefit of the child, and in the claim of lineage, there is a benefit that outweighs the benefit that necessitated considering the finder's hand, so it is removed to attain what outweighs the purpose of considering it. But here, the claim of slavehood is not like that; rather, it harms him by changing the attribute of ownership to that of being owned, so it is not removed except with evidence. It is a condition for its acceptance that the witnesses be Muslim, because he is Muslim by the abode and by possession, so judgment against him is not made by the testimony of disbelievers, unless he is considered a disbeliever by being found in a place of the People of the Book, as we explained. In al-Muḥīṭ: If the finder claims that he is his slave but does not admit that he is a foundling, then his word is taken because the minor is in his hand. But if he admits that he is a foundling, then his claim is not believed except with evidence. He restricted it to evidence because he does not become a slave by the admission of the claimant. So if the foundling confirms him before puberty, his confirmation is not effective because it harms him; he cannot hear his own testimony after the ruling of freedom. This is unlike the case where a minor is in the hand of a man, and he claims that he is his slave and the boy confirms him; then he becomes his slave even if he is not discerning, because he was only known in his hand, and if he denies, it is not valid due to the existence of his hand in some respect. If he reaches puberty and admits that he is the slave of so-and-so, and the latter claims him, if it is before a judgment has been passed against him that is only passed against free persons, like the full fixed punishment and the like, his admission is valid and he becomes a slave because he is not accused of self-harm. But if such a judgment has been passed, it is not accepted and he does not become a slave, because it would nullify the ruling of the judge, and because he is contradicted by something established in sacred law, so it is as if the person for whom he admitted slavery denied him. And if the foundling is a woman and she admits to slavery after she has grown up, or after marriage, it is valid and she becomes a slave for the one in whose favor she admitted, but she is not believed in invalidating the marriage because after...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is a ruling with proof, explaining the evidentiary requirements for claiming a foundling as a slave. The commentator expands on the matn's statement 'and he does not become a slave except with evidence.' The foundling is presumptively free, so anyone claiming he is a slave must present clear evidence (bayyina). The commentary addresses a potential objection: that the finder is an opponent (khaṣm) and thus his evidence should not be accepted because it is not against a specific adversary. The response distinguishes two cases: when an outsider claims lineage (nasab), the finder's hand (custody) is removed without evidence because lineage benefits the child and outweighs the finder's interest. But when someone claims the foundling is his slave, that harms the child (changing from free to slave), so the finder's hand is not removed unless there is evidence. Additionally, the witnesses must be Muslim because the foundling is considered Muslim by the abode and by being in Muslim hands; testimony of non-Muslims is not accepted against him, except if the foundling is considered dhimmi by location. The passage then cites al-Muḥīṭ: if the finder claims the foundling is his slave but does not admit it is a foundling, his word is taken (because the child is in his possession). But if he admits it is a foundling, then his claim of slavery requires evidence. The foundling's own admission of slavery before puberty is ineffective because it harms him and he lacks legal capacity. After puberty, his admission is valid only if no prior judgment of freedom (like hadd punishment) has been passed against him; otherwise, the admission is rejected as it would overturn a judicial ruling. The passage ends with a partial discussion on a female foundling admitting slavery after marriage.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyina): Evidence, typically two male Muslim witnesses or one male and two female witnesses, required to prove a claim of slavery against a foundling.
يد الملتقط (yad al-multaqit): The finder's hand: his physical possession and custody over the foundling, which is recognized for the child's benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When can a finder's claim that a foundling is his slave be accepted without evidence?
A) Always, because the finder has possession.
B) Only if the finder does not admit it is a foundling.
C) Only if the foundling is a dhimmi.
D) Never, because the finder's word is always doubted.

Key Takeaway
A foundling is presumptively free; a claim of slavery requires strong evidence, and the finder's admission of foundling status strengthens this requirement.

Returning Luqata: Describing Marks and the Guarantor Requirement
Ruling on returning luqata with description and guarantor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on returning luqata by stating the conditions of describing marks and the requirement of a guarantor.

Core Idea
Accurate description permits return, but the finder is not compelled unless witnesses appear.

This lesson examines the procedure for returning a found object (luqata) to a claimant. You will learn that the finder may only return it if the claimant accurately describes all distinguishing marks. However, a common misconception is that such description compels the finder to return; in fact, the Hanafi school holds that the finder is not forced. Additionally, the lesson covers when the finder should take a guarantor for his own protection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main rulings
First, note that the finder may return the luqata only if the claimant describes its distinguishing marks accurately. Second, the finder should take a guarantor (kafil) from the claimant as a precaution, unless the claimant brings witnesses.
2
Analyze the dispute on compulsion
The passage from Fath al-Qadir presents two views: some say the finder is compelled to return when the claimant describes marks, while others compare it to an agent handling a deposit and say he is not compelled. The Hanafi majority adopts the latter, arguing that the true owner is unknown.
This dispute is resolved by the tarjih in al-Hidayah and Fath al-Qadir, favoring non-compulsion.
3
Apply the condition of full accuracy
The description must match all marks; partial accuracy is insufficient, as stated in previous sections (al-Laqit). This is a strict condition.
4
Determine when a guarantor is needed
According to al-Hidayah, a guarantor is taken when returning based on description, but if the claimant brings witnesses (bayyinah), no guarantor is needed. This is agreed upon (bila khilaf) because the finder takes the guarantor for his own benefit.
5
Consider the edge case of two claimants
The passage notes that if two claimants both accurately describe the marks, it seems permissible to hand over to each, but the author leaves this open as an unresolved issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوجته وولده لو فقيراً. القارورة والجلد يغطي به رأسها والوكاء ككساء رباط القربة وغيرها وقد وكأها وأوكأها وعليها وكلما شد رأسه من وعاء ونحوه وكاء ؛ كذا في القاموس وظاهر مفهوم الشرط أنه لو لم يبين علامتها لا يحل الدفع وهو محمول على ما إذا لم يصدقه فإن صدقه حل الدفع. قال في فتح القدير : فإن صدقه مع العلامة أو لا معها فلا شك في جواز دفعه إليه لكن هل يجبر؟ قيل يجبر كما لو أقام بينة، وقيل لا يجبر كالوكيل بقبض الوديعة إذا صدقه المودع لا يجبره القاضي على دفعها إليه ودفع بالفرق بأن المالك هنا غير ظاهر أي المالك الآخر والمودع في مسألة الوديعة ظاهر .اهـ. وقدمنا حكم ما إذا دفعها بلا بينة ثم أثبتها آخر وهو أعم من دفعها بالعلامة أو بالتصديق فقط . ولم يذكر المصنف أخذ الكفيل عند دفعها ببيان العلامة قال في الهداية : ويأخذ منه كفيلاً إن كان يدفعها إليه استيثاقاً وهذا بلا خلاف لأنه يأخذ الكفيل لنفسه بخلاف الكفيل لوارث غائب عند أبي حنيفة .اهـ. وصحح في النهاية أنه لا يأخذ كفيلاً مع إقامة الحاضر البينة. والمراد ببيان العلامة بيانها مع المطابقة، وقدمنا في اللقيط أن الإصابة في بعض العلامات لا تكفي. وصرح في التتارخانية في التصوير بأنه أصاب في علامات اللقطة كلها. فظاهره أنه شرط ولم أر حكم ما إذا بين كل من المدعيين لها علاماتها وأصابا وينبغي أن يحل له الدفع لهما.
And his wife and his child if poor. The bottle and the skin covering its head and the string like the covering of the water-skin and other things, and he tied it (wakaha) and made it tied (awkaha) and upon it, and whenever he tightened its head from a container etc., it is a string (wika'); thus in al-Qamus. And the apparent implication of the condition is that if he does not describe its distinguishing marks, then it is not permissible to hand it over. This is interpreted as when he does not confirm him; if he confirms him, then handing over is permissible. He said in Fath al-Qadir: If he confirms him with the mark or without it, there is no doubt about the permissibility of handing it over to him, but is he compelled? It was said he is compelled, as if he brought evidence; and it was said he is not compelled, like the agent receiving the deposit when the depositor confirms him, the judge does not compel him to hand it over to him, and he responded with the difference that the owner here is not apparent, i.e., the other owner, while the depositor in the deposit case is apparent. End. And we have already presented the ruling when he handed it over without evidence and then another claimant proved it, and it is more general than handing it over by description or by mere confirmation. And the author did not mention taking a guarantor when handing it over upon description of the mark. He said in al-Hidayah: He takes a guarantor from him if he hands it over to him as a precaution, and this is without dispute, because he takes a guarantor for himself, unlike the guarantor for an absent heir according to Abu Hanifa. End. And in al-Nihayah, it is corrected that he does not take a guarantor when the present claimant brings evidence. And what is meant by description of the mark is description with correspondence. And we have already presented in al-Laqit that accuracy in some marks is not sufficient. And it was explicitly stated in al-Tatar Khaniyyah in the illustration that he was accurate in all the marks of the luqata. So its apparent meaning is that it is a condition, and I have not seen the ruling when two claimants both describe its marks and both are accurate, and it should be permissible for him to hand it over to both.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the conditions for returning a found object (luqata) to a claimant. It begins with lexical definitions: a 'wakaa' is the string or tie used to close a container. The key ruling is that the finder may only return the luqata to a claimant who accurately describes its distinguishing marks. If the claimant does not describe the marks, it is not permissible to return it to him. However, if the finder himself confirms the claimant's truthfulness (even without marks), it becomes permissible. The question arises whether the finder is compelled to return it if he trusts the claimant. According to Fath al-Qadir, there is a difference of opinion: some say he is compelled, like when the claimant brings evidence; others say he is not compelled, comparing it to an agent handling a deposit who is not compelled to return it to a depositor who merely confirms without evidence. The difference is that in the luqata case, the true owner is hidden (potentially someone else), whereas in the deposit case, the owner is known. The passage then addresses the need for a guarantor (kafil) when returning the luqata upon description. According to al-Hidayah, the finder must take a guarantor as a precaution. This is agreed upon (bila khilaf) because the finder takes a guarantor for his own protection, unlike the case of a guarantor for an absent heir. However, if the claimant brings witnesses (bayyinah), no guarantor is needed. The condition for returning by description is that the description must match all the marks exactly; partial accuracy is insufficient, as noted in the section on foundlings (laqit). The passage concludes with an open question: if two claimants both accurately describe the marks, it seems permissible to hand over to both (as each may have a partial claim), though the author has not seen a definitive ruling on this.

Key Terms
اللقطة (al-luqata): A lost item found by someone; the finder is called al-multaqit.
العلامة (al-'alama): Distinguishing marks of the luqata that identify it to the owner.
الكفيل (al-kafil): A guarantor taken by the finder from the claimant as a precaution against future claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the finder of a luqata need to take a guarantor (kafil) from the claimant?
A) When the claimant brings witnesses (bayyinah) to prove ownership.
B) When the claimant describes the marks accurately but does not bring witnesses.
C) Always, regardless of evidence.
D) Only when the finder is rich.

Key Takeaway
The finder of luqata may return it to a claimant who accurately describes all marks, but is not compelled; he must take a guarantor unless the claimant has witnesses.

The Finder's Right to Use Luqata: Poor vs Rich
Ruling on finder's use of luqata based on poverty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the finder's use of luqata based on financial status, identify the conditions for permissibility, and explain the opposing Shafi'i view and the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
A poor finder may own the found item; a rich finder must give it in charity – permission for the poor is default, no qadi needed.

The finder of a lost item (luqata) faces a crucial question: can he use it for himself? The Hanafi school draws a sharp line based on the finder's wealth. A poor finder may own and spend the item after the announcement period, while a rich finder must give it in charity. This lesson unpacks the evidence from hadith, the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, and an internal Hanafi debate about whether qadi permission is required.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its condition
Identify the act (finder using luqata) and the key condition: the finder's poverty. Note that for a poor finder, it is permissible (mubah) to own the item; for a rich finder, it is prohibited (haram) and he must give it in charity.
The text explicitly says: 'yuntafi'u biha law faqiran wa illa tasaddaq'.
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith cited: 'If its owner does not come, then give it in charity.' This is the foundational proof. Also note the hadith of Abu (رضي الله عنه) used by al-Shafi'i.
Dalil is from Sunnah, ahad hadith.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the hadith supports the Hanafi position: 'give it in charity' implies it must go to the poor, not to a rich finder. The rich are excluded because charity is for the needy.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub; here the command to give charity indicates obligation on the rich finder.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
State al-Shafi'i's position that even a rich finder may benefit, based on the hadith of Abu (رضي الله عنه). Then present the Hanafi response: that hadith is interpreted as allowing only for the poor or as being with the Imam's permission (as a loan).
The Hanafi response uses ta'wil (interpretation) to reconcile the texts.
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on qadi permission
Note that the majority of Hanafi scholars (Hidayah, Nahr, Fath) hold that a poor finder does not need qadi permission. Some (Bishr, al-Khaniyyah) require it. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leans to the majority but notes the contrary.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: no permission required for the poor.

قوله : ( وينتفع بها لو فقيراً وإلا تصدق على أجنبي ولأبويه وزوجته وولده لو فقيراً) أي ينتفع الملتقط باللقطة بأن يتملكها بشرط كونه فقيراً نظراً من الجانبين كما جاز الدفع إلى فقير آخر، وأما الغني فلا يجوز له الانتفاع بها، فإن كان غير الملتقط فظاهر للحديث فإن لم يجئ صاحبها فليتصدق بها والصدقة إنما تكون على الفقير كالصدقة المفروضة وإن كان الملتقط فكذلك. وقال الشافعي : يجوز لقوله عليه السلام في حديث أبي رضي الله عنه «فإن جاء صاحبها فادفعها إليه وإلا فانتفع بها وكان من الأغنياء ولأنه إنما يباح للفقير حملاً له على رفعها صيانة لها والغني يشاركه فيه ولنا أنه مال الغير فلا يباح الانتفاع به إلا برضاه
