The Retriever's Liability and the Condition of Witnessing
Commentary on matn: 'If it runs away from the retriever, he is not liable'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of witnessing that makes the retriever not liable for the slave's loss and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on this requirement.

Core Idea
Witnessing the intention to return transforms the slave into a trust; without it, the retriever is liable.

This lesson examines the rule that a retriever of a runaway slave is not liable if the slave runs away from his custody. The key is the condition of witnessing at the time of taking; this creates an amanah. The compensation (ja'ala) is also tied to this condition. We will explore the core ruling, the intra-Hanafi difference, and how compensation is affected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states: 'If the slave runs away from the retriever, he is not liable.' Identify the act (taking the runaway), the ruling (no liability for loss), and the condition (witnessing intention to return).
The condition 'if he witnessed' is explicit in the sharh.
2
Understand the basis: amanah
The sharh explains: 'because it is a trust in his hand if he witnessed that he took it to return it.' Recognize that the retriever becomes a trustee, not a guarantor, when he manifests his intent.
Compare with a borrower: a trustee is not liable for loss without negligence.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on witnessing
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (عندهما) require witnessing for non-liability and entitlement to compensation. Abu Yusuf holds that witnessing is not required for non-liability; the retriever is a trustee regardless.
This dispute also applies to lost property (luqta) as referenced in the text.
4
Recognize the effect on compensation
If the retriever does not witness, he is not entitled to ja'ala (compensation) and is liable for the slave. If he witnesses, he gets compensation and no liability for loss.
The compensation is proportional to the distance traveled (per day).
5
Apply to a scenario
Take the case: A retriever takes a runaway, witnesses his intention, travels one day, then the slave runs away. The retriever is not liable for the loss but loses compensation for the days not completed.
This leads to the detailed rules on partial travel covered in the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإن أبق من الراد لا يضمن) لأنه أمانة في يده إذا أشهد أنه أخذه ليرده كما سيأتي. ولم يذكر سقوط الجعل قالوا: ولا جعل له لأنه في معنى البائع من المالك ولهذا كان له أن يحبس الآبق حتى يستوفي الجعل بمنزلة البائع، يحبس المبيع لاستيفاء الثمن، وكذا إذا مات في يده لا شيء له ولا عليه ولو أعتقه المولى كما لقيه صار قابضاً بالاتفاق كما في العبد المشترى، وكذا إذا باعه من الراد لسلامة البدل له والرد وإن كان له حكم البيع لكنه بيع من وجه فلا يدخل تحت النهي الوارد عن بيع ما لم يقبض فجاز ؛ كذا في الهداية. وقوله كما لقيه ليس بقيد بل لو أعتقه بعد ما سار به الراد ثلاثة أيام أو أكثر ليرده ثم أبق بعده فإن الجعل لا يسقط كما صرح به في المحيط بخلاف ما إذا سار به أقل من ثلاثة أيام. وقال أبو حنيفة : إن كان المولى دبره ثم هرب فلا جعل له لأن بالتدبير لم يزل الرق. وسبب الاستحقاق هو الرد إلى المولى في حالة الرق ولم يرده اهـ. ولم يذكر المصنف حكم ما إذا رده آخر بعدما أبق من الأول، وذكر في المحيط أن الأول إذا أدخله المصر فهرب منه فأخذه آخر ورده إلى مولاه فلا جعل لواحد منهما، وإن خرج من المصر ورده الثاني من مسيرة سفر فله الجعل، ولو أخذ الآبق من مسيرة سفر فسار به يوماً ثم أبق منه متوجهاً إلى بلد مولاه ولا يريد أن إلى مولاه، فإن أخذه الذي كان أخذه ثانياً يرجع فسار به اليوم الثالث فرده فله ثلثا الجعل جعل اليوم الأول والثالث، فإن أخذه مولاه أو العبد إلى مولاه فلا جعل للآخذ لأنه لم يدفعه إلى مولاه، ولو كان العبد لم يأبق ولكن فارقه وجاء إلى مولاه متوجهاً لا يريد الإباق فللآخذ جعل يوم لأنه لم يتمرد منه بل منقاد له فلم تنقطع يده عنه فصار كأنه رده إلى مولاه، ولو أخذ عبداً آبقاً من مسيرة سفر فسار به يوماً ثم دفعه إلى آخر أو باعه منه أو وهبه وسلمه وأمره أن يدفع إلى مولاه فدفعه أو سار العبد بنفسه فللآخذ جعل اليوم الأول ولا شيء للمدفوع إليه اهـ. ۲۷۲ كتاب الإباق ويشهد أنه أخذه ليرده أي يشهد الآخذ للآبق . ولو قال إن أشهد أنه أخذه ليرده لكان أولى ليكون شرطاً لعدم ضمانه بإباقه من يده فإن الإشهاد لنفي الضمان آخذه عن شرط عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف كما تقدم في اللقطة لكن لم يعلقه به ليفيد أن الإشهاد شرط لاستحقاق الجعل أيضاً حتى لو رده من لم يشهد وقت الأخذ لا جعل له عندهما لأن تركه الإشهاد أمارة أنه أخذه لنفسه فصار كما إذا اشتراه من الآخذ أو اتهبه أو ورثه فرده على مولاه لا جعل له لأنه أخذه لنفسه إلا إذا أشهد أنه اشتراه ليرده فيكون له الجعل وهو متبرع في أداء الثمن. واتفقوا أنه لو أقر أنه أخذه لنفسه فلا جعل له والحاصل أنه إن أشهد أنه أخذه ليرده استحق الجعل وانتفى الضمان عنه بموته وإباقه وإلا لا لكن ينبغي أن يكون الإشهاد شرطاً لهما عند التمكن، أما إذا لم يتمكن منه فلا اتفاقاً كما تقدم نظيره في اللقطة، وأن القول قوله في أنه لم يتمكن منه، ثم رأيت التصريح به في التتارخانية
His statement: (If it runs away from the retriever, he is not liable) because it is a trust in his hand if he witnessed that he took it to return it, as will come. And he did not mention the dropping of the compensation. They said: He has no compensation because he is in the meaning of a seller from the owner; therefore he has the right to detain the runaway until he recovers the compensation, like the seller detains the sold item to recover the price. Similarly, if it dies in his hand, he gets nothing and owes nothing. If the owner emancipates it when he meets it, he becomes a taker by agreement, as in the purchased slave. Likewise, if he sells it to the retriever for the safety of the price to him, and returning it has the ruling of a sale in one respect, so it does not fall under the prohibition of selling what one has not taken possession, so it is permissible. Thus in al-Hidaya. His statement 'when he meets him' is not a restriction; rather, if he emancipated after the retriever had traveled with it three days or more to return it, then it runs away afterwards, then the compensation is not dropped as explicit in al-Muhit, unlike if he traveled with it less than three days. Abu Hanifa said: If the master had made it mudabbar then it ran away, there is no compensation because tadbir did not remove the status of slavery, and the cause of entitlement is returning it to the master in a state of slavery, and he did not return it. He did not mention the ruling if another returns it after it ran away from the first. In al-Muhit, if the first brought it into the city and it ran away from him, then another took it and returned it to its master, there is no compensation for either. If it left the city and the second returned it from a travel distance, he gets the compensation. If someone took a runaway from a travel distance and traveled with it one day, then it ran away from him heading towards the master's town not intending to go to its master, then the one who had taken it second took it again and traveled the third day and returned it, he gets two-thirds of the compensation: compensation for the first and third day. If the master took it or the slave went to the master by itself, the taker gets no compensation because he did not deliver it to the master. If the slave did not run away but separated from the taker and came to the master heading towards him not intending to run away, the taker gets compensation for one day because he did not rebel but was compliant, so the taker's control did not cease, and it is as if he returned it to the master. If someone took a runaway from a travel distance and traveled with it one day, then handed it over to another, or sold it to him, or gifted it and handed it over, and ordered him to deliver it to the master, and he delivered it, or the slave went by himself, the taker gets compensation for the first day and nothing for the one to whom it was handed over. End.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This lesson covers the commentary on the matn phrase 'If the slave runs away from the retriever, he is not liable.' The sharh explains that the retriever holds the slave as a trust (amanah) if he witnessed his intention, so he is not liable if it runs away. The compensation (ja'ala) is not automatically lost; the retriever has the right to detain the slave like a seller detains goods until paid. If the slave dies in his custody, no claim or liability. If the master emancipates the slave upon meeting, the retriever is considered to have taken possession. The discussion then moves to scenarios where the slave runs away from the first retriever and a second person catches it: if the first had already brought it within the city limits, no compensation for either; if outside the city and at travel distance, the second gets compensation. There are detailed rulings about partial days traveled and the slave's intent. The text also addresses the case where the slave becomes mudabbar: if the master declared tadbir after the slave ran away, Abu Hanifa says no compensation because the slave is still a slave but not returned in a state of full slavery? The commentary also includes a quote from al-Kafi regarding the heir's right, which is a dispute with Abu Yusuf. Finally, the retriever may sell the slave to someone and that person returns it, and the retriever still gets compensation for the first day of custody.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for the value of the slave if lost or damaged.
الأمانة (al-amanah): A trust; the slave in the retriever's custody is treated as a trust, so no liability for loss unless negligence.
الجعل (al-ja'ala): The compensation for the retriever, proportional to the distance traveled and time spent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The retriever gets full compensation for the two days.
B) The retriever loses all compensation.
C) The retriever gets half compensation.
D) The retriever is liable for the slave.

Key Takeaway
Witnessing is a necessary condition for both non-liability and entitlement to compensation in the Hanafi school when retrieving a runaway slave.

Reward for Capturing a Runaway Slave: When the Captor Transfers Custody
Ruling on the captor's reward when he transfers the slave to another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the reward (ja'l) for capturing a runaway slave when the captor transfers the slave to another person, and identify the condition that determines the captor's entitlement.

Core Idea
The captor's reward is for the initial capture, not the final delivery — passing the slave does not forfeit it.

In Hanafi law, a person who captures a runaway slave is entitled to a reward (ja'l) for his effort. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies what happens if the captor, after traveling with the slave for a day, hands him over to another person who eventually returns him. The ruling establishes that the original captor retains the reward for the first day, while the second person receives nothing. This principle underscores that the reward is tied to the initial act of securing the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their actions
Who is the captor? What does he do? Who is the second person? Who does what?
The captor takes the slave from a journey distance, travels one day, then transfers to another.
2
Determine the duration of the captor's control
How long does the captor travel with the slave? The passage specifies 'one day'. This is a key condition for the reward.
Less than a day might not trigger the reward; the text implies a minimum of one day.
3
Apply the ruling to the captor
Does the captor receive reward? For what period? The ruling says he gets the reward for the first day.
The reward is for the first day only, not for the entire journey.
4
Apply the ruling to the second person
Does the person who receives the slave from the captor get any reward? The ruling says nothing is due to him.
Even if he physically returns the slave, he gets nothing because the initial capture was already rewarded.
5
Explain the legal rationale
Why does the first captor get the reward? Because his hand was established over the slave and he performed the critical act of taking control.
The slave was submissive (munqad) to him, so his hand was not cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل منقاد له فلم تنقطع يده عنه فصار كأنه رده إلى مولاه، ولو أخذ عبداً آبقاً من مسيرة سفر فسار به يوماً ثم دفعه إلى آخر أو باعه منه أو وهبه وسلمه وأمره أن يدفع إلى مولاه فدفعه أو سار العبد بنفسه فللآخذ جعل اليوم الأول ولا شيء للمدفوع إليه اهـ.
Rather, he is submissive to him, so his hand is not cut off from him, and it is as if he returned him to his master. And if he took a runaway slave from a journey's distance and traveled with him for a day, then handed him over to another, or sold him to him, or gifted him and delivered him, and ordered him to deliver him to his master, and he delivered him, or the slave went by himself, then the taker has the reward of the first day, and nothing is due to the one to whom he was handed over. End.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the reward (ja'l) for capturing a runaway slave. The author clarifies that if the captor takes the slave with the intention to return him, but then passes the slave to another person—whether by sale, gift, or simply handing over—the original captor is entitled to the reward for the first day only. The person who subsequently takes possession and returns the slave to the master gets no reward. This is because the first captor already performed the critical act of taking control. The ruling applies even if the slave returns on his own after the first day: the reward for the first day goes to the first captor. The key principle is that the reward is for the initial act of securing the slave, not for the final delivery.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'l): Reward or compensation given to the person who captures and returns a runaway slave.
آبق (abiq): A runaway slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person captures a runaway slave and travels with him for a day, then sells him to someone else who returns him to the master, who is entitled to the reward (ja'l)?
A) The buyer who returned the slave
B) The original captor for the first day only
C) Both share equally
D) No reward because the slave was sold

Key Takeaway
The reward for capturing a runaway slave belongs to the initial captor for the first day, even if he transfers the slave to another.

The Requirement of Witnessing for Reward and Liability
Expansion of a ruling on witnessing for captive runaway slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad from Abu Yusuf regarding the requirement of ishhad for a captor of a runaway slave, and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Witnessing at capture is a condition for both reward and immunity; without it, you are liable and get nothing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a ruling in Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the captor of a runaway slave. The commentator clarifies that making witnesses (ishhad) at the moment of capture is not merely recommended but is a condition for two things: entitlement to the reward and exemption from liability if the slave runs away. An intra-Hanafi dispute exists: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold it is a condition for both, while Abu Yusuf holds it is only for reward but not for liability. The relied-upon view is that of the two.

Guided Path
1
Locate the base ruling and its expansion
The base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that the captor should witness. The commentator expands by making witnessing a condition, not just a recommendation, for two purposes: negating liability and earning reward.
2
Map the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that witnessing is a condition for both purposes. Abu Yusuf disagrees on liability but agrees on reward.
Abu Yusuf's view is less stringent: he holds that the captor is not liable even without witnessing.
3
Analyze the effect of non-witnessing
Without witnessing, the captor is liable if the slave runs away and gets no reward. The non-witnessing is seen as evidence he took the slave for himself.
This presumption can only be rebutted if he later witnesses that he bought the slave to return him.
4
Apply the exception for inability
If the captor could not find witnesses, the condition is waived by consensus. His word is accepted regarding inability.
This exception is analogous to the rule in luqata (found property).
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: witnessing is a condition for both reward and immunity.
This is the view adopted for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويشهد أنه أخذه ليرده أي يشهد الآخذ للآبق . ولو قال إن أشهد أنه أخذه ليرده لكان أولى ليكون شرطاً لعدم ضمانه بإباقه من يده فإن الإشهاد لنفي الضمان آخذه عن شرط عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف كما تقدم في اللقطة لكن لم يعلقه به ليفيد أن الإشهاد شرط لاستحقاق الجعل أيضاً حتى لو رده من لم يشهد وقت الأخذ لا جعل له عندهما لأن تركه الإشهاد أمارة أنه أخذه لنفسه فصار كما إذا اشتراه من الآخذ أو اتهبه أو ورثه فرده على مولاه لا جعل له لأنه أخذه لنفسه إلا إذا أشهد أنه اشتراه ليرده فيكون له الجعل وهو متبرع في أداء الثمن. واتفقوا أنه لو أقر أنه أخذه لنفسه فلا جعل له والحاصل أنه إن أشهد أنه أخذه ليرده استحق الجعل وانتفى الضمان عنه بموته وإباقه وإلا لا لكن ينبغي أن يكون الإشهاد شرطاً لهما عند التمكن، أما إذا لم يتمكن منه فلا اتفاقاً كما تقدم نظيره في اللقطة، وأن القول قوله في أنه لم يتمكن منه، ثم رأيت التصريح به في التتارخانية
His statement: 'And he witnesses that he took him to return him' means that the taker makes witnesses for the runaway slave. If he said 'I make witnesses that I took him to return him' it would be better so that it is a condition for non-liability if the slave runs away from his hand. For the witnessing is to negate liability; the taker has it as a condition according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf, as already mentioned in the chapter of Luqata. But he did not tie it to that to indicate that witnessing is also a condition for entitlement to the reward, so even if one returned the slave without having witnessed at the time of taking, he gets no reward according to the two, because his leaving off witnessing is an indication that he took him for himself. So it becomes like if he bought him from the taker, or was gifted him, or inherited him, and then returned him to his master: he gets no reward because he took him for himself, unless he witnessed that he bought him to return him, then he gets the reward, but he is a volunteer in paying the price. And they agreed that if he confessed that he took him for himself, then no reward for him. The conclusion: if he witnessed that he took him to return him, he deserves the reward, and liability is negated from him upon his death or running away; otherwise not. But witnessing should be a condition for them (the two) when possible. As for when he is not able, then it is not a condition by consensus, as the analogue was already mentioned in Luqata. And the saying is his saying that he was not able. Then I saw explicit mention of it in al-Tatarkhaniyyah.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the requirement of making witnesses (ishhad) at the time of capturing a runaway slave. The base text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that the captor should witness that he took the slave to return him. The commentator explains that making witnesses serves two purposes: (1) to negate the captor's liability if the slave runs away from his custody, and (2) to establish entitlement to the reward (ja'l). According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), witnessing is a condition for both. Abu Yusuf disagrees regarding liability: he holds that witnessing is not a condition for negating liability. The commentator notes that if the captor does not witness at the time of taking, it is presumed he took the slave for himself, thus no reward is due even if he later returns him. However, if he later confesses that he took it to return him, that confession does not entitle him to reward. The only exception is if he purchased the slave with the stated intention to return him, then he gets reward but is a volunteer in paying the price. The commentator also clarifies that the condition of witnessing applies only when possible; if the captor was unable to make witnesses (e.g., no one present), then the condition is waived by consensus, and his word is accepted regarding inability.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Making others witness that one took the runaway slave with the intention to return him to his master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the consequence if a captor fails to make witnesses at the time of capturing a runaway slave?
A) He is still entitled to reward if he returns the slave.
B) He is liable if the slave runs away and gets no reward.
C) He is not liable but gets reward.
D) He is liable but gets reward.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, making witnesses at the time of capturing a runaway slave is a condition for both entitlement to reward and exemption from liability; the relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

On Whom the Reward Falls: The Principle of Benefit in Returning a Runaway Slave
Expansion of the ruling on ja'l through multiple scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general principle that the reward (ja'l) for returning a runaway slave is borne by the beneficiary of the return, and apply it to different scenarios (mortgage, gift, homicide, etc.).

Core Idea
The reward for returning a runaway slave falls on the one who benefits from the return, not the owner indiscriminately.

In Hanafi fiqh, the concept of ja'l (reward) for returning a runaway slave is not a fixed charge on the owner. Instead, it is tied to the benefit (manfa'ah) that accrues from the restoration of the slave's value. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq explains how this principle applies across a variety of situations—mortgaged slaves, split ownership, trading slaves, and more—revealing the subtlety of Hanafi reasoning in allocating obligations based on real benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The overarching principle is that the reward (ja'l) is owed by the person who benefits from the return of the runaway slave. 'Benefit' here means the restoration of a right or value that the person had in the slave.
This is the key to understanding all the following scenarios.
2
Analyze the mortgage scenario
The mortgagee (creditor) benefits because the slave secures his debt. If the slave's value exceeds the debt, the reward is split: the mortgagee pays only the portion corresponding to his debt, and the mortgagor pays the rest, as the mortgagor's right is revived for the excess.
This shows that the obligation is proportional to the benefit.
3
Examine split ownership (corpus vs. usufruct)
When one person owns the corpus (raqaba) and another the usufruct (khidma), the reward falls on the beneficiary of the usufruct, because the return primarily benefits that person's right to the slave's service. After the service period ends, the usufructuary can recover the ja'l from the corpus owner or the slave.
Benefit is immediate and direct.
4
Consider the trading slave and homicide cases
For a trading slave (ma'dhun) in debt, the master chooses either to pay the debt and keep the slave (then reward on him) or to sell him (then reward from the sale price). For a slave who committed wrongful homicide (khat'), the reward is on the one who chooses to ransom him (master or heirs), because the ransomer benefits by saving the slave's life for his own use.
The master's election determines the beneficiary.
5
Apply to stolen and gifted slaves
A usurper (ghasib) who benefits from the slave's possession must pay the reward. For a gifted slave, the donee is the owner at the time of return and benefits, so he bears the reward regardless of whether the gift is later revoked.
The benefit is measured at the moment of return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وجعل الرهن على المرتهن) لأنه أحيا ماليته بالرد وهي حق المرتهن إذ الاستيفاء منها والجعل في مقابلة إحياء المالية فيكون عليه. أطلقه فأفاد أن الرد في حياة الراهن وبعده سواء لأن الرهن لا يبطل بالموت لكن يرد على إطلاقه ما إذا كانت قيمته أكثر من الدين فليس الكل عليه فإنما عليه بقدر دينه والباقي على الراهن لأن حقه في القدر المضمون فصار كثمن الدواء وتخليصه من الجناية بالفداء. وأشار بوجوبه على المرتهن الذي ليس بمالك للرقبة لكون المنفعة عائدة إليه لكونه مضموناً عليه إلى أن العبد الموصى برقبته لإنسان وبخدمته لآخر إذا أبق فالجعل على صاحب الخدمة لأن المنفعة له، فإذا انقضت الخدمة رجع صاحب الخدمة على صاحب الرقبة أو العبد فيه، وإلى أن المأذون المديون لو أبق فأداء الجعل على من يقع الرد له وهو من يستقر الملك له فإن اختار المولى قضاء دينه كان الجعل عليه وإن اختار بيعه كان الجعل في الثمن يبتدأ به كما أسلفناه ولا شيء على المشتري، وإلى أن الآبق لو كان خطأ لا في يد الآخذ فإنه على من سيصير له إن اختار المولى فداءه فهو عليه لعود منفعته إليه، وإن اختار دفعه إلى الأولياء فعليهم لعودها إليهم، فلو دفع المولى الجعل وأخذه ثم قضى عليه بدفعه إلى الأولياء فله الرجوع على المدفوع إليه بالجعل كما لو باعه القاضي في الدين فإن المولى يأخذ جعله الذي دفعه من ثمنه؛ كذا في المحيط. قيدنا بكونه خطأ لأنه لو كان قتل عمداً ثم رده فلا جعل له على أحد. وقيد بكون الجناية لم تكن وهي في يده إذ لو جنى الآبق في يد الآخذ فلا جعل له على أحد، ولو جنى بعد إباقه قبل أن يأخذه فإن قتل فلا شيء له، وإن دفع إلى الولي فعليه الجعل؛ كذا في المحيط. فجنايته على ثلاثة أوجه كما علمت، وإلى أن العبد المغصوب لو أبق من غاصبه فالجعل على الغاصب. ودل بمفهومه أنه لو رد الموهوب فالجعل على الموهوب له سواء رجع الواهب في الهبة بعد الرد أو لم يرجع لأن المالك له وقت الرد المنتفع به إنما هو الموهوب له ولو وهبه للآخذ، فإن كان قبل قبض المولى فلا جعل وإلا فعلى المولى بخلاف ما إذا باعه منه فإن الجعل له مطلقاً؛ كذا في المحيط.
His statement: 'And the reward is on the mortgagee' because he revived its value by the return, and it is the right of the mortgagee since collection is from it, and the reward is in exchange for reviving the value, so it is upon him. He made it absolute, so it indicates that the return during the life of the mortgagor and after his death are the same because the mortgage is not voided by death. But its absolute nature is restricted to the case where the value is more than the debt: then not all of it is upon him; rather, only the amount of his debt is upon him, and the remainder is upon the mortgagor because his right is only in the amount secured. So it becomes like the price of medicine and ransoming him from injury by ransom. And he indicated by its obligation on the mortgagee who is not the owner of the corpus that the benefit returns to him because it is guaranteed to him. And to the case that a slave whose corpus is bequeathed to one person and his service to another runs away: the reward is on the owner of the service because the benefit is for him. When the service ends, the owner of the service can recover from the owner of the corpus or from the slave. And to the case that a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade) who is in debt runs away: the payment of the reward is on the one to whom the return accrues, i.e., the one who will ultimately own him. If the master chooses to pay his debt, the reward is on him; if he chooses to sell him, the reward is taken from the sale price first, as we previously stated, and nothing on the buyer. And to the case that the runaway slave was involved in a wrongful death (khat') not in the possession of the taker: then it is on the one to whom he will belong. If the master chooses to ransom him, it is on him because the benefit returns to him; if he chooses to hand him over to the heirs, it is on them because the benefit returns to them. So if the master paid the reward and took him, then it was decided that he must hand him over to the heirs, he can recover the reward from the one to whom he was handed over, just as if the judge sold him for debt: the master takes back the reward he paid from its price. This is in al-Muhit. We restricted it to khat' because if it was intentional murder, then there is no reward on anyone. And we restricted by the condition that the injury was not committed while in his hand: if the runaway slave committed a crime while in the hand of the taker, then no reward on anyone. And if he committed a crime after his running away but before the taker took him: if he was killed, then nothing for him; if he was handed over to the guardian, then the reward is on him. This is in al-Muhit. So his crimes are of three types, as you know. And to the case that a stolen slave runs away from the usurper: the reward is on the usurper. And by its implication, if he returns a gifted slave, the reward is on the donee, whether the donor revoked the gift after the return or not, because the owner at the time of return who benefits is the donee. And if the donee gifted him to the taker: if it was before the master took possession, then no reward; otherwise, it is on the master, unlike if he sold him to him: then the reward is for the taker unconditionally. This is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion of the statement from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: 'And the reward is on the mortgagee.' The commentator explains that the reward (ja'l) for returning a runaway slave falls on the person who benefits from the return. In the case of a mortgaged slave, the mortgagee benefits because the slave is security for his debt; by returning the slave, the mortgagee revives its value as collateral. Therefore, the reward is on the mortgagee, not the mortgagor. However, if the slave's value exceeds the debt, the reward is apportioned: the mortgagee pays only the portion corresponding to his debt, and the remainder is on the mortgagor. The commentator then extends this principle to various cases: (1) a slave whose corpus is bequeathed to one and usufruct to another: reward on the beneficiary of usufruct; (2) a trading slave in debt: reward on the master if he pays the debt, otherwise from sale price; (3) a slave guilty of wrongful homicide (khat'): reward on the one who chooses to ransom him (master or heirs); (4) a stolen slave: reward on the usurper; (5) a gifted slave: reward on the donee. The passage also clarifies exceptions: if the slave commits a crime in the hands of the captor, no reward; if intentional murder, no reward; etc. This demonstrates that the reward is a compensation for the benefit of restoring the slave's value to the one who has a right in it.

Key Terms
جعل (ja'l): Reward payable to the person who returns a runaway slave, borne by the one who benefits from the return.
مرتهن (murtahin): The mortgagee (creditor who holds the slave as security).
رهن (rahn): Mortgage or pledge.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who wrongfully takes possession of another's property.
موهوب له (mawhub lahu): Donee; the recipient of a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave is mortgaged for a debt of 100, but his value is 300. He runs away and is returned. The captor is paid a reward of 30. How much does the mortgagee pay?
A) 30 (full amount)
B) 10 (one-third)
C) 20 (two-thirds)
D) 0 (the mortgagor pays all)

Key Takeaway
The reward (ja'l) for returning a runaway slave is an obligation on the person who derives actual benefit from the return, not merely the nominal owner.

Maintenance and Other Rulings on Runaway Slaves
Rules on maintenance, detention, theft, and hiring of a runaway slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on maintenance of a runaway slave, identify the condition for reimbursement, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting the hand for theft.

Core Idea
A runaway slave's maintenance is like a found item: reimbursement only with court permission and explicit intention.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains the legal status of a runaway slave ('abaq) in Hanafi fiqh. The slave's maintenance is analogized to a found item (luqata), with nuances in detention and punishment. Understanding these rules is crucial for anyone dealing with lost or stray property in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling on maintenance
The passage states that the ruling on maintenance of a runaway slave is like that of a found item. If the captor spends without judge's permission, he is a volunteer (cannot claim reimbursement). With permission, he may recover if he explicitly states his intention to recover.
The condition 'with the intention of recovering' is the correct view.
2
Distinguish detention from luqata
Unlike a found item, the judge does not hire out the runaway slave; instead, he detains the slave as a ta'zir (deterrent). The maximum detention period is six months, after which the judge sells the slave.
Expenses during detention come from the public treasury.
3
Identify the dispute on theft punishment
The passage notes a difference with Abu Yusuf: the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) holds that a runaway slave's hand is not cut for theft until his master appears. Abu Yusuf disagreed.
This shows intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Summarize other rulings
Emancipation of a runaway slave is valid, even if as expiation for zihar. If someone hires the slave, the wages belong to the captor, who must give them in charity; but if paid to the master, it is permissible by istihsan.
These rulings appear in al-Tatarkhaniyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وأمر نفقته كاللقطة) أي وحكم نفقة الآبق كحكم نفقة اللقطة لأنه لقطة حقيقة، فلو أنفق عليه الآخذ بلا أمر القاضي كان متبرعاً، وبإذنه كان له الرجوع بشرط أن يقول على أن يرجع على الأصح وله أن يحبسه للنفقة الدين، فإن طالت المدة ولم يجئ صاحبه باعه القاضي وحفظ ثمنه كما قدمناه وأسلفنا أن القاضي لا يؤجره بخلاف اللقطة وأنه يحبسه تعزيراً له بخلاف الضال. وقدر في التتارخانية مدة حبسه بستة أشهر ثم يبيعه بعدها قال : وينفق عليه مدة الحبس من بيت المال. وسيأتي حكم بيع الآبق وهبته في البيوع الفاسدة وإعتاقه جائز ولو عن كفارة ظهار، ولا تقطع يده بسرقة تثبت عليه حتى يحضر مولاه خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وإن أجره رجل فالأجر له ويتصدق به وإن دفعه إلى المولى كان له حلالاً استحساناً؛ كذا في التتارخانية والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
His statement: 'And the matter of his maintenance is like the luqata' means that the ruling on the maintenance of a runaway slave is like the ruling on the maintenance of a found item because he is essentially a luqata. So if the taker spent on him without the judge's permission, he is a volunteer. With his permission, he has the right to recover, on the condition that he says 'with the intention of recovering' according to the most correct view. And he may detain him for the maintenance debt. If the period becomes long and his owner does not come, the judge sells him and keeps his price as we have preceded. And we have already stated that the judge does not hire him out, unlike the luqata, and that he detains him as a deterrent, unlike a stray animal. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah the period of his detention is set at six months, then he sells him after that. He said: 'And he spends on him during the detention period from the public treasury.' And the ruling on the sale and gift of a runaway slave will come in the chapter on invalid sales. And his emancipation is valid, even if as expiation for zihar. And his hand is not cut off for theft proven against him until his master appears, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And if a person hires him, the wages are for him (the taker) and he gives them in charity; and if he pays them to the master, it is permissible for him by istihsan. This is in al-Tatarkhaniyyah. And Allah is All-Knowing.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage covers the maintenance of a runaway slave, treating it analogously to a found item (luqata). The captor may spend on the slave's upkeep only with the judge's permission; without permission, he is a volunteer and cannot claim reimbursement. With permission, he may recover the expenses on the condition that he explicitly states his intention to claim reimbursement (the correct view). He may also detain the slave as security for the maintenance debt. If the master does not appear after a long period, the judge sells the slave and preserves the price. The commentator notes a difference from luqata: the judge does not hire out the runaway slave but detains him as a deterrent. The period of detention is set at six months in al-Tatarkhaniyyah, after which the slave is sold, and during detention, expenses are from the public treasury. The passage also touches on other rulings: (1) emancipation of a runaway slave is valid, even as expiation for zihar; (2) the hand of a runaway slave is not cut off for theft until the master appears, contrary to Abu Yusuf; (3) if someone hires the runaway slave, the wages belong to the taker, who must give them in charity, or if he gives them to the master, it is permissible by istihsan. These rulings demonstrate the unique legal status of the runaway slave as an object of reward and maintenance.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance expenses for the runaway slave, including food, clothing, etc.
لقطة (luqata): A lost item found by someone; the finder has certain duties and rights.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment; here, detaining the runaway slave as a deterrent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when can a runaway slave's hand be cut for theft?
A) Immediately upon proof of theft
B) Only after the master appears and is present
C) Only after the slave is returned to the master
D) Never, because a slave cannot be punished

Key Takeaway
A runaway slave's maintenance is analogous to a found item, requiring court permission for reimbursement; detention is a deterrent; and theft punishment is delayed until the master appears.

Definition of the Missing Person and Appointment of a Guardian
Defines mafqud and explains judge's role in appointing guardian

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the mafqud (missing person), state the key criterion for his legal status, and explain the judge's obligation to appoint a guardian for his property.

Core Idea
A missing person's status hinges on unknown life/death, not location; the judge must safeguard his property like that of an incapable.

When someone vanishes without trace, Islamic law does not presume immediate death. Instead, it grants a dual status: alive for his own benefit, dead for others' benefit. This lesson unpacks that definition and explains the judge's duty to appoint a manager for his estate, ensuring preservation until certainty arises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition
Find the key phrase: 'absent whose whereabouts are unknown' and note that the criterion is ignorance of life/death, not location. The example of a captive in dar al-harb illustrates this.
The term 'mafqud' is broader than simply 'lost' — it covers anyone whose life status is unknown.
2
Distinguish immediate vs. future rulings
The text presents two rulings: for the present, the mafqud is alive regarding his own rights (wives stay married, estate not divided) but dead regarding others' rights (no inheritance from him). For the future, after a set period, he is declared dead.
This dual status is unique and practical — it protects both the missing person and those who would claim against him.
3
Understand the judge's appointment of a guardian
The judge must appoint someone to 'take his right, preserve his wealth, and manage it'. This duty arises because the mafqud is akin to a child or insane person — incapable of managing his own affairs.
The guardian's role includes collecting yields and debts, but not necessarily filing lawsuits.
4
Clarify the role of an existing agent (wakil)
If the mafqud had already appointed an agent for preservation, that agent remains valid and the judge should not appoint another. However, an agent for receipt from the owner (a personal agent) may not have litigation authority.
This preserves the missing person's prior arrangements while allowing court intervention only when necessary.

The Book of the Missing Person. He is an absent one whose whereabouts are unknown. So the judge appoints someone to take his right, preserve his wealth, and manage it. From his loss: he loses him, loss. So he is a lost one and missing; as in al-Qamus. His saying: (and he is absent whose whereabouts are unknown) means his life and death are not known. The criterion is ignorance of his life and death, not ignorance of his place. They have included from it — as in al-Muhit — the Muslim captured by the enemy whose life or death is unknown even though his place is known, namely dar al-harb. It is general, whether it is known that he is in a specific town of dar al-harb or not. The summary of what the author mentioned of his rulings is that he has two rulings: a ruling for the present and a ruling for the future. The basis in the first is that he is alive with respect to himself: his wealth is inherited from him? (text difficult), his wives are not married, and dead with respect to others: so no one inherits from his wealth and it is not divided among his heirs until his death is proven by evidence or he reaches an age that the author will explain. As for the future ruling, it is the judgment of his death after the passage of a specific period. His saying: (the judge appoints someone to take his right, preserve his wealth, and manage it) because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for every incapable of managing his own affairs, and the missing person is of this description. He becomes like a child and a madman. Appointing a preserver for his wealth and a manager for him is for his benefit, but only when needed. So if he had an agent (wakil) and then goes missing, it is appropriate that the judge does not appoint an agent because the agent is not dismissed by his client's absence if he was an agent for preservation, as per al-Walwaljiyyah. The classification: a man goes absent and puts his house in the hand of a man to maintain it, or gives his wealth to him to preserve, and the giver goes missing. He must preserve it, and he may not maintain the house except with the judge's permission, because perhaps he died and the man is not an executor. He says: The right is absolute, including assets and debts of yields and others, what was in his house or with his trustees. It is obvious that he collects his yields and debts acknowledged by him because it is from the category of preservation, so he pleads in a debt that became due by his contract. The Book of the Missing Person. His saying: (Because he is not dismissed by the loss of his client etc.) He said in al-Nahr: The apparent is that he does not have the authority to collect his debts that his debtors acknowledged nor his yields, and then he needs the appointment, and this was the secret in their absolute appointment of an agent. And Allah knows best. The Book of the Missing Person because he is an original in his rights, and he does not plead regarding what the missing person undertook nor for a share for him in real estate or goods in the hand of a man because he is not an owner nor a deputy for him; he is only an agent for receipt from the judge, and he does not have the authority to plead without disagreement, and the disagreement is only regarding the agent for receipt from the owner in a debt.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines the missing person (mafqud) and outlines the judge's duty to appoint a guardian for his property. The author begins by giving the lexical definition: one who is lost and cannot be found. The legal definition hinges on ignorance of whether the person is alive or dead, not on ignorance of his location. For example, a Muslim captive in dar al-harb whose status is unknown is considered mafqud even though his location is known. The ruling on the missing person is twofold: an immediate ruling and a future ruling. In the immediate sense, he is considered alive for his own benefit (his wives cannot remarry, his property is not inherited) but dead for the benefit of others (no one inherits from him, his estate is not divided). The future ruling is that after a certain period, he is legally declared dead. The judge must appoint a guardian to protect his property, similar to a child or insane person. If the missing person already had an agent (wakil) for preservation, that agent remains valid and the judge should not appoint another, as per al-Walwaljiyyah. The guardian's authority extends to collecting yields and debts, and he can plead in court for debts that are due. However, if the missing person had an agent for receipt from the owner (i.e., a personal agent), the issue is disputed: does that agent retain authority to sue? The text notes that there is no disagreement that the court-appointed guardian cannot sue (since he is not a litigant), but there is disagreement about the personal agent's ability to sue.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): The missing person; one whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is not known.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge who appoints a guardian for the missing person's property.
الوكيل (al-wakil): An agent appointed by the missing person before his disappearance, or by the judge after.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The mafqud is defined by unknown life/death, not unknown location; the judge must appoint a guardian to protect his property, but an existing preservation agent remains valid.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Qada ala al-Ghaib
Intra-Hanafi dispute on enforceability of qada ala al-ghaib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi narrations regarding the enforceability of a judgment against an absentee (qada ala al-ghaib) and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each.

Core Idea
Two Hanafi narrations exist on qada ala al-ghaib: one enforces, one requires another judge's approval.

This passage delves into an internal Hanafi dispute: when a judge rules against a missing person after appointing a guardian, is that judgment enforceable? The answer reveals a fascinating tension between two authoritative narrations, each favored in different chapters of fiqh. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for a mufti to know which ruling to apply in cases involving missing persons.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact legal question: Is a judgment rendered against an absentee (qada ala al-ghaib) enforceable without the approval of another judge?
2
Identify the two narrations
Summarize each position: (1) Enforceable if the judge deems it; (2) Not enforceable except by sanction of another judge.
These are known as al-riwayatan.
3
Trace the proofs
What is the basis for each? The first says the ijtihad is about the cause (hujjiyyat al-bayna); the second says it is about the judgment itself.
The analogy of the flogged judge is used.
4
Locate the contextual preference
Note which narration is preferred in each chapter: In Bab al-Mafqud, enforceability; in Kitab al-Qada, non-enforceability.
Also note the confusion about the meaning of 'qadi' (judge).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In missing person cases, the guardian can obtain a binding judgment against a debtor; in general judgments, a second judge's approval is needed.
This affects how a mufti advises in real cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا كان كذلك تضمن الحكم به قضاء على الغائب وأنه لا يجوز إلا إذا رده القاضي وقضى به لأنه مجتهد فيه؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد عليه أن المجتهد فيه نفس القضاء فينبغي أن يتوقف نفاذه على إمضاء قاض آخر كما لو كان القاضي محدوداً في قذف . أجيب بأن المجتهد فيه سبب ا القضاء وهو أن البينة هل تكون حجة من غير خصم حاضر أو لا، فإذا رآها القاضي حجة وقضى بها نفذ قضاؤه كما لو قضى بشهادة المحدود في القذف. واستشكله الشارح بأن الاختلاف إنما هو في نفس القضاء وإلا لم يتصور الاختلاف في نفس القضاء فلا ينفذ حكمه إلا بتنفيذ قاض آخر ولهذا قال الشارح في كتاب القضاء: إن الأصح أنه لا ينفذ إلا بتنفيذ قاض آخر لأن الاختلاف في نفس القضاء وتبعه المحقق ابن الهمام هناك لكن ذكر هنا عن الخلاصة أن الفتوى على النفاذ والحاصل أن في نفاذ القضاء على الغائب روايتين فصححوا في باب المفقود رواية النفاذ وفي كتاب القضاء رواية عدمه لكن وقع الاشتباه بين أهل العصر في المراد بالقاضي على الغائب هل المراد به الأعم من الحنفي وغيره أو المراد غير الحنفي ومنشؤه فهم عبارة الهداية وغيرها هنا حيث قالوا : إذا رآه القاضي نفذ هل المراد أنه رأي له واعتقاد فيخرج الحنفي لأنه لا يرى القضاء على الغائب أو المراد إذا رآه القاضي مصلحة فقال في العناية إلا إذا رآه القاضي أي جعل ذلك رأياً له وحكم به. وقال في فتح القدير : أي رأى القاضي المصلحة في الحكم على الغائب أوله اهـ. وقال الشارحون وصاحب الخلاصة والبزازية في توجيه الجواب عما أورد أن المجتهد فيه نفس القضاء إذا رآها القاضي حجة وقضى بها نفذ وهو موافق لما في العناية المقتضي لتخصيص القاضي بغير الحنفي، ومن العجب ما في الخلاصة من نقل الإجماع على نفاذ القضاء على الغائب لو فعل، وإنما الخلاف في أنه هل يقضي وينصب وكيلاً عن الغائب أم لا، وستزداد وضوحاً في كتاب القضاء إن شاء الله تعالى والحاصل أنه لا تسمع الدعوى ولا تقبل البينة فيما لو ادعى إنسان على المفقود ديناً أو وديعة أو شركة في عقار أو رقيق أو رداً. أو مطالبة لاستحقاق بعيب لعدم الخصم لأن منصوب القاضي ليس بخصم، وكذا ورثته لأنهم يرثونه بعد موته ولم يثبت، ولم يذكر المصنف بيع شيء من ماله وفي الهداية : : ثم ما كان يخاف عليه الفساد يبيعه
الموفق. قوله : (تضمن الحكم به قضاء على الغائب) قال في الحواشي السعدية : فيه شيء والظاهر أن يقال قضاء للغائب وكتب على قوله وأنه لا يجوز ما نصه في فصل القضاء بالمواريث من شرح الأتقاني وأحال على المختلف أنه قيل يجوز القضاء للغائب عندهما ولا يجوز عنده .
When it is so, the judgment by it entails judgment against an absentee, and that it is not permissible unless the judge confirms it and passes judgment, because it is a matter of ijtihad; as in al-Hidayah. It was objected that the matter of ijtihad is the very judgment itself, so its enforcement should depend on the approval of another judge, as if the judge was flogged for slander. The answer is that the matter of ijtihad is the cause of judgment, that is, whether the evidence is valid without a present opponent or not. So if the judge considers it valid and passes judgment, his judgment is enforceable, as if he passed judgment based on the testimony of one flogged for slander. The commentator found this objectionable, stating that the difference is in the very judgment, otherwise the difference in the very judgment would be inconceivable. So his judgment is not enforceable except by the sanction of another judge. Hence the commentator said in the Book of Judgments: The correct view is that it is not enforceable except by the sanction of another judge, because the difference is in the very judgment. The investigator Ibn al-Humam followed him there, but he mentioned here from al-Khulasa that the fatwa is on enforceability. The conclusion is that there are two narrations regarding the enforceability of a judgment against an absentee. They deemed correct in the Chapter of the Missing Person the narration of enforceability, and in the Book of Judgments the narration of non-enforceability. However, confusion arose among contemporaries regarding the meaning of 'judge against an absentee': does it mean the general, including the Hanafi and others, or does it mean the non-Hanafi? Its source is the understanding of the wording of al-Hidayah and others here where they said: If the judge sees it, it is enforceable. Does that mean it is his opinion and belief, so the Hanafi is excluded because he does not hold the view of judgment against an absentee? Or does it mean that the judge sees it as a benefit? So in al-Inayah he said: Unless the judge sees it, i.e., he makes it his opinion and judges by it. And in Fath al-Qadir he said: That the judge sees the benefit in judging the absentee. The commentators and the author of al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya, in directing the answer to what was objected, that the matter of ijtihad is the very judgment. If the judge considers it as proof and passes judgment, it is enforceable. And this is consistent with what is in al-Inayah, which necessitates that the judge is specifically non-Hanafi. It is surprising that in al-Khulasa it is reported that there is consensus on the enforceability of judgment against an absentee if done. Rather, the disagreement is whether he judges and appoints an agent for the absentee or not. This will become clearer in the Book of Judgments, God willing. The conclusion is that no lawsuit is heard and no evidence is accepted if someone claims a debt, trust, partnership in real estate, slaves, or return, or a claim of defect for lack of a litigant, because the appointee of the judge is not a litigant. Likewise his heirs because they inherit after his death, which is not proven. And the author did not mention selling any of his property, but in al-Hidayah: Then what is feared to spoil, he sells it. Al-Muwaffaq. His saying: (The judgment by it entails judgment against an absentee) He said in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya: There is something in it, and the apparent is that it should be said judgment for the absentee. And he wrote on his statement that it is not permissible, as text in the chapter of judgment on inheritances from Sharh al-Atqani, and he referred to al-Mukhtalaf that it was said: Judgment for the absentee is permissible according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and not permissible according to him (Abu Hanifa).
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the enforceability of a judgment rendered against an absentee (qada ala al-ghaib). The context is a judgment in favor of the missing person's guardian when the guardian sues someone on behalf of the missing person. The issue is whether such a judgment is binding without the opposing party being present. According to al-Hidayah, if the judge passes judgment, it is enforceable because the matter is subject to ijtihad. However, there is an objection: if the ijtihad is about the judgment itself, then its enforcement should require the approval of another judge, just as a judge who has been flogged for slander cannot have his judgment enforced without review. The response is that the ijtihad is about whether evidence can be accepted without a present opponent. If the judge accepts it, his judgment stands. The commentator (al-Marghinani) objects, saying the difference lies in the very ruling, not the cause. Thus, he maintains that the judgment needs another judge's sanction. Various authorities are cited: Ibn al-Humam follows the commentator, while al-Khulasa reports the fatwa is on enforceability. Thus, there are two narrations. In the Chapter of the Missing Person, the preferred view is enforceability; in the Book of Judgments, non-enforceability. This apparent contradiction arises from different interpretations of 'qadi' (judge): some say it refers to any judge (including Hanafi), others restrict it to non-Hanafis because Hanafis generally do not accept judgment against an absentee. The passage concludes that in practice, no claim against the missing person is accepted because the guardian is not a litigant, and heirs are not heirs until death is proven. However, the judge may sell property that is at risk of spoiling.

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qada ala al-ghaib): A judgment issued against a person who is not present and without a representative.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fih): A matter that is subject to ijtihad, i.e., there is no explicit text, so the ruling depends on the judge's reasoning.
الروايتان (al-riwayatan): The two narrations regarding enforceability of qada ala al-ghaib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which statement is TRUE regarding the Hanafi view on qada ala al-ghaib?
A) All Hanafi imams agree it is invalid.
B) The preferred view in Kitab al-Qada is enforceability.
C) The preferred view in Bab al-Mafqud is enforceability.
D) The Hanafi school has no clear opinion.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two valid narrations on qada ala al-ghaib, with contextual preference depending on the chapter (missing persons vs. general judgments).

Limitations of the Representative for a Missing Person
Ruling on the representative's capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exact role of the qadi-appointed representative for a missing person and explain why he cannot litigate.

Core Idea
The representative is only an agent for receipt, not a litigant.

When a person goes missing, the qadi appoints a representative to manage his affairs. But what can this representative do? The Hanafi school is clear: he is not a deputy or owner; he is merely an agent for receiving property. This lesson clarifies his limitations and the underlying legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the representative's capacity
Read the text: 'he is not an owner nor a deputy; he is only an agent for receipt from the qadi.' What does this imply about his authority?
He acts on behalf of the qadi, not the missing person directly.
2
Determine his litigation rights
The text explicitly says 'he does not own litigation without dispute.' Why is he barred from litigating?
He lacks ownership or deputyship.
3
Connect to the concept of asalah
The missing person is 'original in his rights' (asil fi huquqihi). How does that affect the representative's role?
The missing person retains his rights; the representative merely receives.

كتاب المفقود
لأنه أصيل في حقوقه ولا يخاصم في الذي تولاه المفقود ولا في نصيب له في عقار أو في عروض في يد رجل لأنه ليس بمالك ولا نائب عنه إنما هو وكيل في القبض من جهة القاضي، وأنه لا يملك الخصومة بلا خلاف وإنما الخلاف في الوكيل بالقبض من جهة المالك في الدين، وإذا كان كذلك تضمن الحكم به قضاء على الغائب وأنه لا يجوز إلا إذا رده القاضي وقضى به لأنه مجتهد فيه؛ كذا في الهداية. وأورد عليه أن المجتهد فيه نفس القضاء فينبغي أن يتوقف نفاذه على إمضاء قاض آخر كما لو كان القاضي محدوداً في قذف . أجيب بأن المجتهد فيه سبب ا القضاء وهو أن البينة هل تكون حجة من غير خصم حاضر أو لا، فإذا رآها القاضي حجة وقضى بها نفذ قضاؤه كما لو قضى بشهادة المحدود في القذف. واستشكله الشارح بأن الاختلاف إنما هو في نفس القضاء وإلا لم يتصور الاختلاف في نفس القضاء فلا ينفذ حكمه إلا بتنفيذ قاض آخر ولهذا قال الشارح في كتاب القضاء: إن الأصح أنه لا ينفذ إلا بتنفيذ قاض آخر لأن الاختلاف في نفس القضاء وتبعه المحقق ابن الهمام هناك لكن ذكر هنا عن الخلاصة أن الفتوى على النفاذ والحاصل أن في نفاذ القضاء على الغائب روايتين فصححوا في باب المفقود رواية النفاذ وفي كتاب القضاء رواية عدمه لكن وقع الاشتباه بين أهل العصر في المراد بالقاضي على الغائب هل المراد به الأعم من الحنفي وغيره أو المراد غير الحنفي ومنشؤه فهم عبارة الهداية وغيرها هنا حيث قالوا : إذا رآه القاضي نفذ هل المراد أنه رأي له واعتقاد فيخرج الحنفي لأنه لا يرى القضاء على الغائب أو المراد إذا رآه القاضي مصلحة فقال في العناية إلا إذا رآه القاضي أي جعل ذلك رأياً له وحكم به. وقال في فتح القدير : أي رأى القاضي المصلحة في الحكم على الغائب أوله اهـ. وقال الشارحون وصاحب الخلاصة والبزازية في توجيه الجواب عما أورد أن المجتهد فيه نفس القضاء إذا رآها القاضي حجة وقضى بها نفذ وهو موافق لما في العناية المقتضي لتخصيص القاضي بغير الحنفي، ومن العجب ما في الخلاصة من نقل الإجماع على نفاذ القضاء على الغائب لو فعل، وإنما الخلاف في أنه هل يقضي وينصب وكيلاً عن الغائب أم لا، وستزداد وضوحاً في كتاب القضاء إن شاء الله تعالى
Book of the Missing Person: Because he is original in his rights and does not litigate regarding what the missing person undertook, nor regarding a share for him in real estate or in movable goods in the hand of a man, because he is not an owner nor a deputy for him; he is only an agent for receipt from the qadi. And that he does not own litigation without dispute, and the only dispute is concerning the agent for receipt from the owner in a debt. When this is the case, the judgment by it entails a judgment against an absentee, and it is not permissible unless the qadi approves it and judges by it because it is subject to ijtihad; thus in al-Hidayah. It was objected that what is subject to ijtihad is the judgment itself, so its execution should be suspended on the endorsement of another qadi, as if the qadi was hadd-punished for slander. The answer is that what is subject to ijtihad is the cause of judgment, which is whether the evidence is proof without an opponent present or not. If the qadi sees it as proof and judges by it, his judgment is executed, as if he judged by the testimony of the one hadd-punished for slander. The commentator objected that the disagreement is about the judgment itself; otherwise the disagreement in the judgment itself is inconceivable, so his judgment is not executed except by enforcement of another qadi. Hence the commentator said in the Book of Judgments: The correct view is that it is not executed except by enforcement of another qadi because the disagreement is about the judgment itself. The muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam followed him there, but here he mentioned from al-Khulasa that the fatwa is on execution. The conclusion is that there are two narrations regarding the execution of judgment against an absentee; they affirmed in the chapter of the missing person the narration of execution, and in the Book of Judgments the narration of non-execution. But confusion arose among later scholars regarding the meaning of 'qadi' in 'judgment against absentee': is it general, including Hanafi and others, or specifically non-Hanafi? Its origin is the understanding of the wording of al-Hidayah and others here where they said: 'If the qadi sees it, it executes' — does it mean that it is his view and belief, thus excluding the Hanafi because he does not see judgment against absentee, or does it mean if the qadi sees it as a benefit? In al-Inayah: 'unless the qadi sees it' i.e., he made that his view and judged by it. In Fath al-Qadir: i.e., the qadi saw benefit in judgment against absentee, interpret it [as such]. The commentators and authors of al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyyah said in directing the answer to the objection that what is subject to ijtihad is the judgment itself; if the qadi sees it as proof and judges by it, it executes, which agrees with what is in al-Inayah requiring specification of the qadi to non-Hanafi. Amazing is what is in al-Khulasa of reporting consensus on execution of judgment against an absentee if done; the only disagreement is whether he judges and appoints a proxy for the absentee or not. It will become clearer in the Book of Judgments, God willing.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the missing person (mafqud) in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It discusses the status of the representative appointed by the qadi over the missing person's affairs. The author states that this representative is not a true litigant (khasm) because he is merely an agent for receipt (wakil fi al-qabd) from the qadi, not an owner or deputy. Therefore, he cannot litigate concerning property that the missing person had in someone's hand, nor can he claim rights to real estate or movables on behalf of the missing person. This is undisputed; the only dispute is about the agent for receipt from the owner (malik) in debt. The text then moves to the issue of judgment against an absent person (qada' ala al-gha'ib). According to al-Hidayah, such a judgment is not valid unless the qadi approves it and it is subject to ijtihad. An objection is raised that if the subject of ijtihad is the judgment itself, then it should require endorsement by another qadi. The answer is that the ijtihad is about the proof (bayyinah) — whether it is valid without an opponent. The commentator (sharih) objects that the disagreement is about the judgment itself, so a new qadi must execute it. The text then reconciles two narrations: one from al-Khulasa that the fatwa is on execution, and another from the Book of Judgments (kitab al-qada) that it requires another qadi. The confusion among later scholars about whether 'qadi' refers generally to any judge or specifically to non-Hanafis is clarified: the Hanafi position is that they do not judge against absentees unless the qadi sees a benefit (maslahah). The point of ijtihad is whether the evidence is sufficient without a present opponent. The passage concludes that the khilaf is actually about whether the qadi should judge and appoint a proxy for the missing person, not about the validity of the judgment itself. The author promises further clarification in the Book of Judgments.

Key Terms
وكيل بالقبض (wakīl bi al-qabḍ): An agent appointed by the qadi solely to receive property on behalf of the missing person.
أصيل (asīl): The original owner of a right; the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the representative for a missing person cannot litigate?
A) Because the qadi forbids it.
B) Because he is not the owner or deputy of the missing person.
C) Because the missing person's rights are suspended.
D) Because litigation requires a lawyer.

Key Takeaway
The representative for a missing person is strictly an agent for receipt and cannot engage in litigation.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Judgment Against an Absentee
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qada' ala al-gha'ib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi narrations regarding execution of judgment against an absentee and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Two narrations: one allows immediate execution, the other requires another qadi's endorsement.

When a judgment is passed against a person who is not present in court, does it execute immediately or must it be reviewed by another qadi? The Hanafi school has two narrations. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the reasoning, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Read the text: it mentions a narration in the chapter of missing person (execution) and another in the Book of Judgments (non-execution). Which one is cited where?
The dispute is within the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the objection and answer
The text presents an objection: if the ijtihad is about the judgment itself, it should require another qadi. Then an answer: the ijtihad is about the cause (sabab) – the evidence. Compare these positions.
The commentator (sharih) insists the disagreement is about the judgment itself.
3
Analyze the confusion about 'qadi'
Some later scholars misunderstood whether 'qadi' includes Hanafi judges. The text clarifies that the Hanafi qadi must see a benefit (maslahah) to execute.
This nuance affects the practical application.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Khulasa, the fatwa is on execution. Find the supporting phrases.
This is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms
قضاء على الغائب (qaḍāʾ ʿalā al-ghāʾib): A legal judgment issued against a person not present in court.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahad fīhi): A matter subject to ijtihad (independent legal reasoning).

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position on judgment against an absentee, what condition allows the qadi to execute the judgment immediately?
A) The absentee has a representative present.
B) The qadi sees the evidence as proof and judges by it.
C) The case is about property only.
